CALIFORNIA
ARE DELAYED
AT MANILA
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MANILA, May 23.â The departure of
the First California Infantry for home
has been prevented by a typhoon. The
rainy season in fact has already fairly
begun.
Major Bell of the Fourth Cavalry to
day made a reconnoissance at Santa
Rita, west of Bacolod. He encountered
considerable resistance from the enemy
and had several men wounded.
There was also a brush with the
enemy at Mariquina and later at Pa
say, where a line of insurgent skir
mishers who were shielding a force of
trench diggers attacked General Oven
shine's lines. The rebels were repulsed
by a battalion of the First Idaho In
fantry and two companies of the Twen
tieth Infantry. The Americans had two
men killed and two wounded.
These attacks upon the American
forces to-day, while Aguinaldo's com
missioners are in Manila negotiating
for peace are held to show a lack of
control of the rebel chiefs over their
followers and the futility of negotia
tions while armed rebels still keep the
field.
Recent events and negotiations for
peace have not brought about any
change in the American military policy.
So long as rebels appear in arms they
will be attacked and dispersed.
The railway tracks have been re
paired and the first train reached San
Fernando this morning.
The Spanish newspaper, Oceania, has
been suppressed by the military au
thorities and the editor arrested, for
publishing objectionable articles.
Two battalions of the Sixth Artillery
and 260 marines arrived here from San
Francisco to-day. Brigadier General
Williston, who arrived In command of
the Sixth Artillery, will succeed Gen
eral Hughes as provost marshal of
Manila. General Hughes has been as
signed, to duty in the Yisayan Islands.

TO CO ON FIGHTING
Filipino Leaders Believe They Can
Get Better Terms by Continu
ing the War.
Special Cable to The Call and the New Tor*
Herald. Copyrighted, IVM. by James Gor
don Bennett.
MANILA, May 23.â The
Filipino Commissioners
called early to-day upon
General Otis, but made no offi
cial propositions relative to sur
render. They met the American
Commission later and held four
hours' conference.
It is said that the Filipinos
were dismayed at the terms of
fered in President McKinley's
cablegram. Gregorio del Pilar
turned red under his brown skin
when he realized that he was be
ing offered nothing but vague
promises of possible representa
tion for his people in case of
compliance with the American
demand that they lay down
arms.
Every Filipino shrugged his
shoulders as the glowing prophe
cies of the future after the cessa
tion of hostilities were outlined
to them by the members of our
commission. They said that they
asked for independence and lib
erty with dignity, under the pro
tection of the United States, and
were now offered nothing but the
indefinite assurance that individ
ual natives might hold some offi
cial positions under the proposed
government. The native Com
missioners have great dread of fu
ture Congressional action. They
will accept not a single condition
of those proposed, but will re
turn dissatisfied to Aguinaldo
and report.
Major Zailcita, one of the Fili
pino Commissioners, said: "As
the ultimate form of government
rests in the hands of the Ameri
can Congress, the present pro
posals are merely provisional.
The result of the present plan
I would be that Americans would
be at the head of every depart
ment of government, as the Fili
pinos' tenure of office would de
pend on the will of the Ameri
! cans. We think we can get bet
ter, terms."
A leading banker of Manila
jsays: "The present proposals
are only a repetition of the provi
; sions of the American commis
i sion's proclamation. That means
, that this is an American colony.
You have adopted an imperialis
tic policy, then why talk over
j matters with the envoys of a hos
tile force which must be crushed
before such a government as is
proposed can be established?
"The opinion of military offi
cials is that the negotiations
merely encourage the insurgents
to hold out for better terms, espe
cially since the commading
American general is not con
! suited. General Luna is bound
to continue to fight for indepen
dence. Negotiations which en
courage the natives to hope to
obtain great concessions may
cost the lives of many American
soldiers."

few minutes $300 was raised with which
to carry on the preliminaries.
To-night the convention adjourned
after discussing details and directing
that the work of incorporating the
Northern California Fruit Exchange be
proceeded with at once.

hair pulled out and that they were cruelly
treated in various other ways, and pray
ing their fellow countrymen to hurry to
their release.

murdering Chinese who fall into Lneir
hands, and our men, I suppose, should
consider themselves fortunate they have
not been similarly treated, though the
cruelties and Indignities heaped upon
them must, be hard to bear."

PARIS IS DOOMED
Believes That the Attempt to
Float the LiQer Will Be
Unsuccessful.
Â© FALMOUTH, Eng., May 23.â A leading Cornish diver who returned Â£
8? here to-night from the Paris takes a very pessimistic view of the SS
* prospect for saving the liner. He said: Â£g
Â§ "I do not expect that the Paris will be refloated. She is pierced <%
6 by rocks amidships, her foreholds are badly damag-ed, the bulkheads are *8
Â§Â§ practically useless, and even if she were got off the rocks I believe she 2
M would sink. In my opinion the only remedy is a false bottom, which it ct
g would take a month to complete, and I believe that even moderately ib
g rough weather in the meantime would break up the ship."
S Everything is in readiness for to-morrow's effort to float the vessel $
n if the weather continues favorable. 88
8? Another diver reports that the Paris has sustained heavy damage p
S from her bow to a point seventy feet aft, but he thinks the principal
j| damage is inside the double-bottom. gÂ»
PEACE CONFERENCE
CLOSELY GUARDED
THE HAGUE, May 23.â The chiefs of
the delegations to the Peace Conference
held a private meeting this morning at
"The House in the Woods," and after
ward informed their colleagues of
the arrangements made for a choice of
presidents of the committees as agreed
upon last evening.
The selections were ratified without
incident. The following is a list of the
heads of the first committee: Honor
ary presidents, Count yon Munster,
head of the German delegation, and Mr.
Andrew D. White, head of the Ameri
can delegation; effective president, A.
P. C. Van Karnebeck, head of the dele
gation of The Netherlands. This com
mittee is divided into two sections, war
and marine. The vice presidents of
the war section are: Lieutenant Gen
eral Abdulla Pasha of the Turkish dele
gation; Major General Ardagh of the
British delegation and Brigadier Gen
eral Monnier of the French delegation.
The vice presidents of the marine sec
tion are: Vice Admiral Fisher of the
British delegation; Vice Admiral Pe
than of the French delegation and Cap
tain Siegel of the German delegation.
The second, committee is constituted
as follows: Honorary presidents, the
Duke de Tetuan, head of the Spanish
delegation; Turkhan Pasha, head of
the Turkish delegation, and Count
yon Welserschcemb, head of the Aus
tria-Hungary delegation; effective
president, Professor de Maartens of the
Russian delegation. This committee is
also divided into two sections, Red
Cross and Brussels Conference. The
vice presidents of the Red Cross section
are: General Thaulow and Dr. S. Roth,
head of the Swiss delegation. The vice
presidents of the Brussels Conference
section are Professor yon Stengel of
the German delegation and General
Zuccari of the Italian delegation.
The third committee is composed as
follows: Honorary presidents, Count
Nigra, head of the Italian delegation,
and Sir Julian Pauncefote, head of the
British delegation; effective president,
M. Bourgeois, head of the French dele
gation; vice presidents, M. Debille,
head of the Spanish delegation; Baron
d'Estournelles de Constant of the
French delegation; rv>unt de Macedo,
head of the Portuguese delegation;
Herr Mercy, Signor Compili and Dr.
Zorn.
The three committees held their first
sessions this afternoon, these being de
voted solely to inaugural formalities
and an exchange of ideas with regard
to procedure. The^ will meet again on
Friday.
M. de Staale, head of the Russian
delegation and president of the confer
ence, attended the meeting of the ar
bitration committee, sitting next to M.
Leon Bourgeois, head of the French
delegation.
The measures to preserve the secrecy
of the deliberations have been doubled.
Only the delegates and secretaries are
admitted to the precincts of the Huis
Ten Bosch. Both entrances to the gar
dens are closed with barred gates, which
are guarded by sentries under the com
mand of non-commissioned officers in
full uniform. Every applicant for ad
mission is called upon to produce an
orange-colored card issued to the dele
gates and to give the secret pass
word.
CROTHERS HONORED BY
THE STANFORD ALUMNI
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, May 23.â
The alumni held a business meeting this
morning and elected the following offi
cers: President G. E. Crothers, '95;
vice president, J. E. Elmore: secretary,
Mibs Uliian Ray, '97; treasurer, C. E.
The alumni adjourned to the chapel,
and addresses were delivered by Miss
Blanche J. Anderson, A. M.. '98; Oliver
Dale Richardson. A. M.. '94 and G. E.
<'rother<! "fi who has had charge of the
alumni interests in the tax exemption
movement, and who spoke of its progress
and prospects.
The alumni gathered for their fourth
annual banuuet at 13:30 in Rohle Hall
Professor C. R. Whittler. '93. president of
the alumni, presided as toastmaster. and
gave the first toast to " '99." which was
responded to by Ralph Arnold, president
of the class of '99. Dr. O. L. Elliott spoke
of "The Value of Alumni Associations ;
Professor C. E. Cox of "The Alumni Reg
ister"- A. B. Rice, '99, of "Stanford's Vic
tories"- R. W. Hartwell of "Our Boys in
Manila' 1 ; C. K. Field '95. of ''Mrs Stan
ford." and President Jordan of "The New
Dr Jordan spoke of the changes which
were taking place about the university
and of the plans which were being con
sidered for the future. By the recent sale
of property Mrs. Stanford had been able
to put a large amount of money into the
university, as was shown by the new
buildings now being erected. The assem
bly hall had been accepted and the li
brary the gift of Thomas Welton Stan
ford' was well under way. A change
would be made in the buildings, however,
in regard to roofing.
The present roofing, which was a tin
imitation of tiling, would be replaced, he
said by the genuine article, which was
used' in'all other university buildings. The
memorial arch, which was being erected,
would join the assembly hall. To the east
of the new library building would be con
structed for the use of the history, eceon
omics and English departments. Mrs.
Stanford had made every effort to obtain
plans for the new chapel, which will make
ft the most imposing of any college
structure in the United States.
Long rows of Japanese lanterns were
suspended from the arcades of the quad
to-night. The shrubbery and flower beds
were brilliantly lighted and the moon
hung on the edge of dark clouds, which
swept slowly across the sky. The scene
was one of beauty. It. was the night of
the senior promenade. In the center of
the quad, on a stand built for the occa
sion. Bennet's band from the city dis
coursed sweet music. Even,' conceivable
nook was partitioned off and formed into
booths by the young ladies, who there re
ceived their friends and served them with
refreshments as they lounged about on
the divans.

FRENCH PREJUDICE
TOWARD AMERICA
Call Headquarters, Wellington Hotel,
Washington, May 23.
An example of the manner In which
anti-American prejudice is formed in
some portions of Europe has been
brought to the attention of the State
Department in the issue of Le Petit
Marseillaise of Marseilles, France, of
the 7th inst. This paper contains a
full-page illustration, in garish colors,
representing a young girl undergoing
punishment by the whip in America.
The two prominent figures in the pic
ture are the girl, who is undergoing
public flogging, and the Sheriff. The
latter is a tall figure, wearing a red
shirt and black trousers, his right arm,
with which he is wielding a cruel lash,
bared to the elbow, while the girl stands
with her arms in the stocks and body
bare to the hips, receiving the blows
from the whip. The chastisement is
witnessed, apparently with enjoyment,
by a miscellaneous assemblage of old
men, young girls and little children, all
well dressed, while a number of soldiers
stand in the background to support the
proceeding with the official authority of
the United States Government. Ex
plaining the illustration the Marseillaise
says :
"In a country like America, where one
is accustomed to find only progress and
new discoveries, one is very much sur
prised to see a turn backward like pun
ishment by the whip. This retrograde
movement is performed in the State of
Virginia, in which the Legislative Assem
bly has voted a law permitting the ap
plioation of corporal chastisement in pub
lic.
"The first person upon whom this pun
ishment has been inflicted is a young girl
of 18 years of age, who was whipped on
the public square of Manasas.
"If one did not notice the modern dress
of the crowd which was present at the
scene, in the presence of the Sheriff and
representatives of the public force, he
would have thought himself in the mid
dle ages on seeing the^ unhappy girl, naked
to the waist, both hands held in a piece
of plank, while the executor of the law
acquitted himself conscientiously of his
mission."
It is only necessary to say that pun
ishment by flogging is not authorized
by the laws of Virginia, and that the
illustration is purely imaginary.
SUDDEN DEATH OF
CONVICT VASQUES
SAN QUENTIN PRISON, May 23.â The
prison officials are sorely perplexed over
the death at 4 o'clock this morning of
Mose Vrisques, who expired in his cell just
as the prison physician reached the bed
side. Yas<iues for the pact week has been
at work with the road gang employed in
the building of the Tiburon boulevard.
Yesterday afternoon, while unloading
gravel from a construction car, he lost
consciousness and fell to the ground. He
was picked up and carried to the shade
of a tree, where he immediately revived.
He resumed work and nothing was
thought of the incident.
Vasques returned to the prison in ap
parently good health. He was locked up
with the other prisoners as usual and is
not known to have complained of feeling
ill. This morning as the cooks and din
ing room men were released from their
cells groans were heard from Vasques'
cell. A rap at the door brought no re
sponse, and Dr. Lawlor, the prison phy
sician, was summoned. When Lawlor
reached the prisoner's bedside Vasques
was found to be dying and he expired a
few moments later. The Coroner held
an inquest this morning. The jury re
turned a verdict of death from natural
causes.
Vasques has spent much' of his time in
the hospital since his incarceration, being
treated for a pulmonary complaint. The
Vasques family is notorious in the crim
inal annals of this State. At one time
there were the father and three sons at
San Quentin. The father. Mates Vas
ques, died in the penitentiary. The fam
ily was implicated in a number of rob
beries in the southern part of the State
where its name was a terror to stage
drivers and travelers.
"SPIKE" SULLIVAN LOSES.
NEW YORK, May 23.-After twenty
five rounds of vicious fighting at the
Lenox Athletic Club, "Kid" McPartland
was given the decision over "Spike" Sul
livan. Going into the fight on the short
end of the betting, McPartland com
pletely upset the calculations of the
sports He bested his man at every
point of the game and administered to
him the most severe punishment that he
has ever received in his pugilistic career

GAGE DEFENDS HIS
POCKET VETO OF
SUFFRAGE BILL
LOS ANGELES. May 23.â Governor
Gage has written a letter to Dr.
Stephen Bowers, editor of the
California Voice, explaining why he
vetoed Assembly bill No. 438, which
conferred the ballot upon women at
school elections.
First, the Governor declares the act
Is clearly in violation of article 2, sec
tion 1, of the constitution of California,
which reads as follows:
Every native male citizen of the United
States, every male person who shall have
acquired the rights of citizenship under
or by virtue of the treaty of Queretaro,
and every male naturalized citizen hereof,
who shall have become such ninety days
prior to any election, of the age of 21
years, who shall have been a resident of
the State one year next preceding the
election, and of the county in which he
claims his vote ninety days, and in the
â¢â¢lection precinct thirty days, shall he en
titled to vote at ull elections which are
now or may hereafter be authorized by
[aw; provided, no native of China, no
Idiot, no Insane person, no person con
victed of any infamous crime, no person
hir>'after convicted of the embezzlement
or misappropriation of public money, and
no person who shall not be al>l^ to read
the constitution in the English language
and write his name, shall ever everclse
the privileges of an elector in this State;
provided, that the provisions of this
amendment relative to an educational
qualification shall not apply to any per
son prevented by a physical disability
from complying with its requisitions, nor
to any person who now has the right to
vote, nor to any person who shall be 60
years of age and upward at the time this
amendment shall take effect. (Amend
ment ratified at election of November tl,
1894. 1
Governor (Jape says:
The mere fact that the act itself at
tempts to limit the officers for whom
women may vote dues not render it less
â  stitutional, because the right of
suffrage is denied by the constitutional
provision just referred to generally; and
an act of the Legislature would be void
which would attempt to permit the exer
cise of that right in a special case. The
Supreme Court of this State, in the case
of Van Volkenberg vs. Brown, 43d Cali
fornia Reports, page 4:!. decided that
woman was not entitled to the right of
suffrage.
The provision of the old constitution,
in bo far as II used the words "male citi
zen of the United States" was similar to
article 11, Bection I, of the present con
stitution. The court, on page 51 of the re
port above referred t... says: "A State
may deny all her political rights to an

LIFE IMPRISONMENT
FOR JUSTIN BROWN
Slayer of Cain Found Guilty of Mur
der in the First Degree at
Bakersfield.
BAKERSFIELD, May 23.â "We, the
jury, find the defendant guilty of murder
in the first degree and recommend his
punishment at life imprisonment."
This was the verdict of the jury in
the Brown murder case. It was returned
at 6:15 this evening, after the jury had
been out an hour and a quarter. After re
turning to jail Brown remarked:
"That's a long time Lo spend in prison. I
guess the jury did what they thought was
right. I've got it and can't do anything."
Justin Brown is 27 years of age. He
had the friendship of Mrs. Cain, the
widow of his victim, throughout the trial.
She is 37 years old and is the mother of
five children, the eldest. 17 years of age.
Crops in Yolo County.
WOODLAND. May 2.?.â A cool south
breeze Is the prevailing wind, and its ef
fect on growing crops Is very beneficial
Every day it continues will materially
Improve the prospects of a good grain
harvest. The crop of early barley will he
as tine as ever was harvested [ n th a
county. The wheat crop will hardly come
up to the expectations raised by the
March rains, but under the influence of
favorable weather prospects are growing
better.

WOODLAND, May 24.â The last Legis
lature enacted a law authorizing cities of
the fifth class to extend the time for the
payment of building bonds from twenty
to forty years. The City Trustees are
considering a proposition to so extend
the bonded indebtedness. Of course such
extension must be with the consent of the
bondholders.

ULTIMATIM
TO BE SENT
TO MERRIAM
Organized Labor in the Coeur
d'Alenes Crowded Too
Far.
si ial Dispatch to Thf> Call.
BUTTE, Mont., May 23.â The Silver
Bow Trades and Labor Association
adopted the following to-day:
Whereas. A reign of terror prevails in
the town and vicinity of Wardner, Idaho.
brought about by the un-American, tyran
nical and outrageous mandates promul
gated by General Merriam, that have re
sulted in the wholesale and Indiscriminate
arrest of hundreds of American citizens
who are at present confined under condi- j
tions so brutal and unsanitary that it has
resulted in the death of four of thÂ» im
prisoned men; and had these men been of
the brute creation Instead of being hu
man beings we would, nevertheless, be I
Justified In remonstrance against the in
human outrages perpetrated in the name
Of the law; therefore br- it
Resolved, That the Silver Row Trades ]
and I^abor Association, in regular ses
sion convened, recognizing the above
statements to be a conservative presenta
tion of the conditions as they exist at
Wardner. and the result that must neces
sarily follow if such acts go unheeded, I
extends to its fellow workers and the |
citizens of Wardner its sincere sympathy
for the outrages, perpetrated upon them; i
and
Resolved. That we call upon organized j
labor and all lovers of patriotism and jus- j
tice everywhere to hold mass demonstra
tions to denounce General Merriam and j
the War Department behind him. the :
subservient Governor of Idaho and the
sympathetic Governor of Montana, for
the tyrannical treatment of the working- j
men of Idaho and Montana; that we fur- i
thermore call on all workingmen here to ;
use thHr ballots to secure a Government !
that will clip the wings of the military
autocrats and truculent officials.
Resolved. That if the martial law game
to defent the trades union succeeds in
this instance the workingmen will find
cause to formulate certain limits to pre
sent to the Government, beyond which
friends of organized labor will not allow I
any of their friends to go, and. standing
on their constitutional rights, they will
prepare to defend these rights at any
cost.

of the right. The application of the max
im 'expressio unius est exclusio alter
ius' bears with full force upon this pro
vision of the constitution, declaring who
are competent to vote at elections au
thorized by the laws of this State."
In the case of Spencer vs. the Board of
Registration, Ist McArthur. page 169, aris
ing in the District of Columbia, it was
held- by the court that the right of suf
frage is not guaranteed by the United
States constitution to women, and the
court says: "Whfle the conditions of
right have varied in several States and
from time to time been modified in the
same State, the right has uniformly rest
ed upon the express authority of the polit
ical power and been made to revolve with
in the limitations of express law."
Again, in Mino vs. iiappersett, 21 Wal
lace, page IM, the Supreme Court of the
United States, passing upon the question
of woman's right to suffrage, under the
provisions of the constitution of Missouri,
denying that right, held that there was no
inherent right to vote by reason of
woman's mere citizenship and that the
constitution of the State of Missouri, con
fining the right of suffrage to men, was
not in vplation of the I'nited States con
stitution, and that the fourteenth amend
ment to the United States constitution
had no application to the subject of suf
frage The court, in ;â  review of the ques
tion In conclusion says:
"As has been seen, all thp citizens of the
States were not vested with the right of
suffrage. In all save perhaps New Jer
sey this right was only bestowed upon
men, and not upon all of them. Under
these circumstances it is certainly now
too late to contend that a Government is
not republican within the meaning of the
guaranty In the constitution because
women ' are not mad.' voters. â¢ * *
Women were excluded from suffrage in
nearly all the States by thp express pro
vision of the constitution and laws."
In conclusion the Governor comments
as follows:
These are a few of the most objection
able reasons which presented themselves
to me at the time of the submission of
the bill for action. I therefore felt it my
duty under the law to refuse to sign a
bill' which was clearly invalid, and which,
moreover, would not have reached the
end desired. ,
In view of what has been hereinbefore
stated, you will readily sec that permit
ting the' exercise of suffrage by females
in respect to school matters could result
in great financial harm to the various
School districts, touching the validity of
their bonds by reason of the doubt as to
their constitutional right to act. As be
fore indicated, I have not here at
tempted to go Into all the details which
Influenced my judgment in the premises,
but from tlie foregoing I am satisfied
that you will coincide thormighlv with
the accuracy of my position. Yours very
respectfully, HENRY T. GAGE.

Judge and Mrs. Joseph H. Budd of

STOCKTON. May 23.â A social event
which will ".waken widespread interest
will occur "ii tl"' evening of June 5 at the
Budd mansion on North Butter street. It

ding of Judj;.- and Mrs. Joseph 11. Budd. j
The Invitations are already out and are I
models of the printer's art. The mono
gram "B" in old English in at the center
and top, while on either side are the dates j
IM:i-1S:io, a'l th e work Oelng in a bright

Surrounded by their children, one of
them o Cornier Governor of the State, the
couple will repeat the vows made fifty
years ago in Wisconsin, when as youth
and maiden they joined their fortunes.
In the throng which will assemble will
be persons from all parts of the State.
Mrs. Budd has for years been famed for
her wifely virtues, but with their exer
cise she has found time to be so active
in all social and charitable movements
as to gain a name that is not limited to
this city, where she has lived so many
years. Judge Budd is one of the best
lawyers on the coast, and has frequently
been honored by his fellow citizens with
high judicial offices. At present he is
Berving his tenth year on the Superior
belich. The years have been full of hon
ors and rewards to the couple, and in
their passing have left so little impression
upon them that the Judge and his wife
will be among the gayest and most youth
ful in spirit of those who gather on the
auspicious occasion.

Bubonic Plague Spreading.
HONGKONG. May 23.-The bubonic
plague Is Increasing. The official reports
thus far for the month of May show 413
cases and 254 deaths.
COAST LINE TO
BE COMPLETED
All Obstacles Are Now
Removed.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA BARBARA. May 23.â 1t now
seems that all obstructions to the com
pletion of the Coast road have been dis
posed of. Some time ago L. G. Dreyfus
announced that a satisfactory agreement
had been made with him by the Southern
Pacific Company. To-day the last of the
obstructionists, the Hollister estate, was
placated. The arch that the Hoilisters
demanded from the railroad company in
front of their ranchhouse (there is a long
fill of forty-two feet directly in front of
the ranch buildings, obstructing the view
and necessitating a grade in order to' get
out of the ranch), and which Superinten
dent Hood estimated would cost $4400, is
to be put in. Hood In his figures evidently
made a mistake and upon investigation
it is ascertained that the cost will not
exceed $2i.iou. The company will agree to
build this and the Hoilisters will grant
the right of way for a distance of .twenty
seven miles through their ranch.
These farts were made known at to
day'a railroad mass-meeting, called by
Hon. Ellwood Cooper. The opera-house
was crowded by property-owners along
the coast line and delegates from the sev
eral counties. Hon. Marion Cannon, ex
rongressniiin from this district, led the
delegates from the outside counties.
Chairman Ellwood Cooper made a short
address, in which he outlined the purpose
of the meeting. He said:
This call has not for its object the increase
of the income of the Southern Pacific Com
pany, but it is to help ourselves. If we want
the "railroad, we want it quickly; and as intel
liKent business men we should do everything in
our power to insure rapid work. If we stop
I*, consider the circumstances or motives that
have induced the railroad company to build
this line, or reflect upon past grievances,
whether they be real or imaginary, we make a
serious mistake. These questions are beyond
our power to know. VW do know, however,
what our duty is, and that is to do everything
in our power to Insure the completion at the
earliest possible moment. Our interests do not
slop even at the completion of the gap. We
will want accommodation facilities to trans
port our products, passenger accommodations,
the good will of the company, harmony In all
our dealings with them. The company is, mi
the ground with all the paraphernalia for rapid
work: it Ib ready to place camps on every
ranch where there Is much work to be done,
and to push It to every extent with a reason
able degree of economy. We must bestir our
selves to insure these facilities.
Ex-Congressman Cannon, hearing that
the people of Santa ISarbara County Itad
agreed to give the Southern Pacific Com
pany a free right of way through the
entire county and including free depot
sites and other such privileges was much
surprised and perplexed. He thought the
company was now receiving too much
from the citizens. He spoke at some
length. A committee of five property
owners was appointed and given power
to investigate and if possible settle all
grievances on the part of property hold
ers along the line. After a greal deal
of discussion the following resolution was
adopted:
Resolved That we. the citizens of the city
and â¢ ounty "t Santa Barbara, in convention
assembled this 23.! day of May. 1899, hail with
pleasure and unfeigned satisfaction the deter
mlnation "f the Bouthern Pacific Company to
Immediately (lose the gap in the coaat road
between Surf and Elwood.
Resolved, also. That we earnestly request
that tlii- parties from whom the right of way
committee have secured either contracts or
,| is give to the railroad company, without
hindrance, their cordial support in furtherance
of the company's efforts to hasten the work of
finishing the road, which means so much to
tli>> future of onr city and county.
Resolved, further. That in view of the fact
that thi j railroad company has commenced in
earnest t" finish the fail to Elwood, we ask
OUT people t" cease criticisms of the company's
actions, and, laying aside all bickerings, to
Join bands in a supreme effort to finish the
work so auspiciously begun.
There is a decided feeling of content
ment here to-day ami every one believes
that the road will DOW be rushed to com
pletion. Contractors Ramish and Marsh
are expected daily from Los Angeles witu
200 men and as many teams. Contractor
McCormick returned from Los Angeles
to-night, where he engaged many more
te&ms to add to his camp at Elwood.
COOLIES TO BE ADMITTED.
Hawaiian Cabinet Reconsiders Appli
cations of Planters.
HONOLn.r. May 16.â The Cabinet held
a meeting this morning with the Bureau j
of Immigration. It was decided first of
all to reconsider the application of Bee
croft, Waialua, Onomea and Pioneer
mills for laborers. It was against these
estates that the Inspector of Immigrants
had filed objections either for sanitary j
reasons or for ill treatment of laborers by j
lunas. The decision was that upon a
shewing by those plantations that the
evils complained of had been remedied
the number of laborers required would be
permitted.
A regulation was made that in future
where reports of the Immigrant Inspector
were unfavorable for sanitary or other
reasons copies of such reports should be
furnished to the agents of the plantation,
with the Information that no other labor
ers would be permitted until the evils
were corrected. The lists of laborers were
looked over ;'tid 5311 were permitted for
the next period.

TAKES LIFE IN
JEALOUS RAGE
Crime of a Mexican at
Bakersfield.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BAKERSFIELD, May 23.â Maria
Barrera, a Mexican woman, was mur
dered in this city early this morning by
her rejected lover, Francisco Ochoa.
Ochoa shot and seriously wounded an
unoffending man named Smith, who
chanced to cross his path just before
he broke in the door of the woman's
home to murder her. Ochoa escaped.
The affair occurred in the tenderloin,
near the corner of L and Twenty-first
streets, at 1 o'clock this morning. Ochoa
and the woman had lived together for
some time. A month ago she cast him
off and his anger prompted the crime.
Ochoa entered the woman's house by
the back door and found Maria Barrera
and another woman in bed. They had
heard the shot tired at Smith and ex
tinguished the light in their room.
Ochoa crashed In the door with his
pistol and attacked Miss Barrera. She
begged plteously, but he fired a bullet
into her breast, death resulting almost
Immediately. Ochoa wore a mask, but
was recognized by the other woman.
Ochoa lied and has since eluded pur
suit. He is about five feet six inches
tall and weighs 130 pounds.
Shortly In-fore the killing Ochoa and
a tamale peddler known as Tomas were
j seen lurking in the back yard of the
I murdered woman's house. Tomas has
i disappeared. It is said Tomas urged
Ochoa to slay the girl.
Smith was shot in the neck. The bul
let lodged near the spinal column and
he may die. He was passing the Bar
rera place when a man thrust a pistol
into his face and fired.' It was Smith's
hallooing that attracted the attention
of the officers. The woman's death was
not discovered until several minutes
after Smith was found.

SEEK EQUITABLE
FREIGHT TARIFFS
Fight Waged by Coast
Jobbers.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
ST. PAUL, Minn., May 23.âRepre
sentatives of the jobbing interests of
the Pacific Coast and the important
commercial centers of the Middle West
met in a conference in the Ryan Hotel
to-day, the result of which will greatly
affect transcontinental shipping busi
ness. The subject of discussion %vas
the transcontinental freight tariff put
into effect by the two northern roads
on May 1, which the Western men
claim will ultimately cause them se
vere loss. Jobbers of the Middle West,
<m the contrary, hold that they cannot
afford to see the tariff changed, and
the opposing factions are arguing the
matter before representative officials o-f
the Western roads. Two sessions were
held to-day, an adjournment without a
decision having been taken at 5 o'clock.
A third session will convene to-morrow
morning.
The coast jobbers represent that if
i the present tariffs are continued it will
ultimately result in curtailing their
trade. They desire a greater difference
| between carload and less than carload
rates and the prohibition of "mixed
carload" shipments. They maintain
'â  that unless a greater difference is made
j in carload and less than carload rates
they fuv certain to lose trade in their
immediate territory, to which they hold
I they are justly entitled.
The contest, though entirely friendly
between the representatives of the two
j interests, is nevertheless close and is
I being waged in earnest. Strong dele
gations were here from San Francisco,
Tacoma, Seattle arid other coast
I points to urge a change of tariff, while
| St. Louis, Chicago, St. Paul and Duluth
I were equally well represented by dele
gations intent upon securing the main
tenance <# the present freight rates.
About eighty jobbers in all were rep
resented. They were met in St. Paul
by J. M. Hannaford. vice president of
the Northern Pacific: Francis B.
Clarke, traffic manager of the Great
Northern; Freight Agent Somers of the
| Great Northern and other prominent
officials.

HELPLESS BABE
EXPOSED TO THE
WIND AND BAIN
Brutal Act of a Mys
terious Stranger.
"Tmi ought to be lynched for keeping
that poor babe on the streets a night like
this," shouted Charlie Stuart at a well
dressed stranger who stood at the junc
tion of Market, Powell and Eddy streets
last night with a three months old baby
in his arms. He had been standing there
since S o'clock, and it was midnight when
Stuart, who Is a prominent traveling man,
accosted him. "I am waiting for my
wife," meekly replied the stranger, but
the nervous twitching at the corners of
his mouth and the cold look he gave the
baby when he uttered the few words gave
the'lie to his statement. The little suf
ferer was blue with cold and wet to the
skin, for the pitiless rain drops had been
beating down on its uncovered face since
9 o'clock. The child's soulful sobs at
tracted the attention of everybody who
passed, but the heartless wretch who held
it in his arms paid little heed to its wails.
The people coming from the theaters saw
the mysterious man with the crying babe
in his arms, and not a few of them, think
ing he was a beggar, slipped him change,
which he eagerly crammed into his
pockets.
Stuart, who had noticed the stranger
walking up and down Eddy street for an
hour or more, became enraged and or
dered him off the street under tho pen
alty of being arrested.
A crowd quickly gathered, and were it
not for the fact that the brute had the
I baby in his arms lie would have been
soundly thrashed. lie made his escape on
the Powell-street car. Stuart also beard
ed the car and rode as far as Ellis street,
: where he called a policeman. Stuart mi
i forme.l the policeman that the man had
kept the babe exposed in the rain for six
hours and then left the car. The police
man accompanied the stranger several
! blocks but did not get his name or place
I him under arrest.
Stuart will visit police headquarters to
day and swear out a "John Doe" warrant
I for the man's arrest.

ACTIVE WORK
IN BEHALF OF
DEWEY STATUE
The General Committee Fixes $100,000
as the Limit of Cost and Appoints
Sub=Committees.
ONE HUNDRED THOUSAND DOL
LARS is the amount the committee
to erect a monument to the Ameri
can navy in commemoration of the
victory of Admiral Dew'ey at Manila has
fixed upon as necessary for its purpose,
and it will proceed at once to raise that
sum. That the committee will be suc
cessful there is not the slightest doubt.
Gratifying progress was made at the
meeting of the committee in Mayor Phe
lan's private office yesterday afternoon.
Besides the Mayor, who is chairman of
the committee, there were present: Irv
ing M. Scott, F. W. Van Slcklen, R. P.
Schwerin, Captain Fletcher, E. W. Hop
kins. Leon Sloss, Horace G. Platt. J. B.
ReiaÃ.ein, Charles L. Patton. C. H. Bund
shu.v'olonel Charles A. Murphy and E.
B. Pond. Little time was wasted in dis
cussion, the members getting right down
to work as soon as Mayor Phelan rapped
for order.
The first matter taken up was the elec
tion of treasurer. At the preliminary
meeting it was suggested that some bank
be selected to act in that capacity, but on
second consideration the members decid
ed that It would be preferable to have one
of their own number hjave control of the
funds, and ex-Mayor E. B. Pond was
unanimously chosen for the position.
The question of finances was then taken
up, the discussion extending to the cost of
the statue. . It was finally decided that
$100,000 would be ample to erect as im
posing and artistic a monument as could
be desired.
A resolution was adopted appointing a
committee of five to prepare and issue an
appeal to the people setting forth the in
comparable service rendered the entire
Pacific Coast by Dewey and his fleet and
the reasons why the public should honor
the great admiral in the manner proposed.
The address will be prepared to-day and
printed in all the papers to-morrow morn
ing.
A second committee was appointed to
wait upon the leading citizens of this city
and ascertain what financial assistance
they will give the movement, as it was
deemed advisable to start the fund with
some larce subscriptions before beginning
1893 or the act of 1897. Section 6S of the
consolidation act Is inconsistent with
â¢ and it therefore stands.
By authority of that section all ordi
nances granting special privileges or
franchises coming within that category
must be submitted to the Mayor. It may
be that bids art absurdly low for valu
able franchises. In that event it would
be my duty to return the order without
my approval.
"There is no disposition certainly on
â  my part to stand in the way of granting
reasonable franchises under ordinary <ir
cumstano s, but as the charter goes into
effect on the Ist of January, 1 think that
all franchiw s hereafter should be
granted under the provisions of that in
strument. 1 am disposed to believe that
when the present street railway fran
s hi San Francisco expire the people
will want to control the streets and the
! street transportation on their own ac
: count by municipal ownership, giving a
< very low rate of fare to passengers in
order to facilitate intercommunication
and thus build the city and bring the
suburbs within the reach of the mechanic
and the laborer. When the laborers were
working on the Dewey boulevard they had
to pay one-tenth of their day's earnings
to come and go to and from their work.
"The anxiety of the Geary Street Rail
way Company, controlled by the Market
I Street Railway, to secure a new franchise
is due. first, to a desire to avoid
the provisions of the new char
ter, which limits the term of a
franchise to twenty-five years and
provides that at the expiration of that
time the roadbed shall revert to the city;
and secondly, the company is in a diffi
culty, which reflects bad management or
bad'faith upon somebody. It is this:
"While the franchise expires in 1903. the
company has issued $Â»571 .0"Â»o worth of
bonds which do not mature until 1921, and
no provision, as T understand it, has been
made for a sinking fund. As a result the
bondholders, who must have bought
â  blindly, will have nothing to show for
' their Investment unless the same com
pany secures the franchise, in addition
to the bonds there is a million dollars'
worth of stock issued at $100 par value.

$37 of which only was paid in. I cannot

chise Is renewed. This reminds me that
the Market street railway stock has been
advancing- rapidly on its earning capacity,
whereas at the expiration of its several
franchises the bondholders will alone be
protected so far as an inadequate Finking
fund can protect them, and the stockhold
ers will get nothing-.
"I do not like to see this stock sold
freely on the market to Innocent pur
chasers, which enables the corporation
when it gets into trouble to cry that it is
owned by "widows and orphans.' I think
innocent purchasers should be warned.
"As for the Geary street franchise, you
will see from the facts that if the new
franchise be offered for sale the com
pany will have to secure it if it desires
to protect the bonds: or the bondholders
themselves will bid and hence the city

Supervisors granting franchises and spe
cial privileges."
Another thing that will make the road
of the transgressor hard for the Mar
ket Street Company is the fact that it
has not relinquished It? Gearv street
franchise. It has gone to the Board of
Supervisors and asked it to grant a fran
chise over a line that until 1903 at least
is held from the public. It asks the
Board of Supervisors to grant a franchise
that will not be in the power of any
Board of Supervisors to grant until ISO 3.
True, it offers to surrender the franchise,
but it does so with a string. "If you give
us fifty yean for four, well trade." How
can the Market Street Railway and the
Board of Supervisors get over the fact
that there is nothing to give and tint if
there was the effort to steal twenty-five
years of life from one provision of the
new charter must prove abortive? It will
try to show It at the next meeting of the
Street Committee on Thursday. After
that, the Mayor.
The other attempts of the Market Street
Company to squeeze the city In its coils,
made at the same time as the applica
tion for the new Geary street franchise,
are no less insidious. A block here and
a block there it asks throughout the city,
ostensibly for the purpose of connecting
Its various lines, but absolutely for the
purpose of taking up at the minimum of
cost streets which if unhampered by
these petty franchises might prove de
sirable ways over which competing com
panies might desire to run lines. They
are all attempts at steals as is the Geary
street application and just as baldfaced.
They should all meet with the same fate.
It is up to Mayor Phelan to stop them
If Supervisors can be found willing
enough to cotintenance them.

The Sunnyside Republican Club last
night passed a unanimous resolution in
dorsing The Call for its action in promot
ing the erection of a statue to Admiral
Dewey to be placed in a commanding po
sition on the Pacific Coast on the shores
of the bay of San Francisco.
At a meeting of the Point .Lohos Im
provement Club last night a resolution
was introduced by Colonel C. H. Murphy,
who claims to be the originator of trie
Dewey statue. The resolution, which is
as follows, was unanimously adopted:
"Whereas. There is a public movement
on hand to erect a statue in this city to
the American navy to commemorate *Ad â 
miral Dewey's great achievement in Ma
nila: and whereas, said naval achieve
ment cleared the Pacific of a fleet that
menaced this coast, and opened the sea
to our commerce; and whereas, San
Francisco and other seaport cities of Cali
fornia are under exceptional obligations
to Admiral Dewey: therefore be it
'â¢Resolved. That the Point T>obos Im
provement Club, moved by sentiments of
gratitude and patriotism, commends this
movement and pledges its support to the
Major and the committee in charge of
said proposition: and be it further
"Resolved, That we highly regard this
project of the people's gratitude for our
national hero; be it further
"Resolved. That we believe it to be the
duty of all good citizens of California to
aid this movement by both moral and
financial assistance."
On the question of selecting a site for
the statue. Secretary YV. P, Johnston of
fered a resolution favoring Sutro Heights
as being the best adapted for the brave
Dewey. From this place it was held that
the statue would be one of the first ob
jects as also thp last seen by all coming
In or going: out of the Golden Gate.
Colonel Murphy had no doubt hut the
Sutra heirs would sell the heigrhts to the
city at a reasonable figure for such a pur
pose.
ROOKERIES ON
MARKET STREET
IN A BIG BLAZE
A Fire That Threat
ened Danger.
CAUSED BY CROSSED WIRES
FRIGHTENED LODGERS LEAVE
IN HOT HASTE.
A Bunch of Tottering Eyesores in
the Business Section Damaged
to the Extent of
$5000.
A fire which started in a mmiwt man
; nr>r and at first seemed insignificant
| threatened the very heart of the city be
' tween the hours of 11 and 12 o'clock last
nischt. At Market and Fourth streets
there are a number of old wooden build
ings, rickety and worm-eaten. The gTound
j floor stores, however, bring in a nice
! rental, and this is probably all that has
| saved them from being torn down and
replaced by modern structures. Last
night, shortly after 11 o'clock, the electric
! light wires became crossed at Fourth and
'â  Market streets. There was a sputtering
of flame on the pole and a sharp hissing
sound could be heard on the sidewalk.
A few minutes later smoke was seen
issuing from the roof of the three-story
frame structure at 7K3 Market street, and
an alarm was turned in from box 98.
When the firemen arrived they could only
locate a blind fire in the building, and
! they started in to fight it. The building
was but a tinder-box, however, and In a
â  few minutes flames were bursting from
' the roofs of the buildings at 3, 5, 9 and 11
I Fourth street. It looked for a time as
though a second alarm would be neces
sary, but the firemen finally managed to
get the blaze under control.
The lire created a great deal of excite
ment along Market street. It was fought
wholly from the south side of the street
and the Market-street cars were not
stopped, but hundreds who were on their
way home jumped off the cars, attracted
!by the blaze. The upper floors of the
buildings affected by the fire were occu
pied as lodging-houses, and the lodgers
made hasty exit, dragging out trunks and
satchels filled with their belongings or
rushing pellmell through the streets with
their arms full of clothing. The building
in which the fire originated immediately
adjoins the notorious Midway Plaisanco.
The show was in progress at the time, but
the noise of a fire engine puffing In front
of the house soon drove the audience from
the building.
The buildings damaged by fire and
water were as follows: 783 Market street

will reach at least $5000.
The Court Erred.
The Judge of the Superior Court of Mon
terey County received a decided setback
yesterday at the hands of the Justices of
the Supreme Court. Charles L. Westlake
in 1597 was the Tax Collector of Monterey
County, and the was indicted by the
Grand Jury for embezzling $371, and after
a trial was sentenced to ten years in the
State prison. An appeal was taken from
the judgment on the ground that the
court had erred in its instructions to the
Jury. Supreme Court Commissioner
Cooper, who wrote the opinion, also con
sidered that a serious error had been com
mitted to the injury of the defendant, for
he holds that "the Judge of the lower
court instructed the jury that the willful
omission to pay over the money itself
made the defendant guilty as charged."
The Justices agreed with this view of the
matter and the judgment and order deny
ing a new trial was reversed.

TO REVISE
THE SYSTEM
OF TAXATION
Revenue Laws to Be
Overhauled.
AN IMPORTANT INNOVATION
FIRST SESSION OF THE SENATE
COMMITTEE.
Outline of the Work Discussed and
Plans of Procedure Decided
Upon by the Com
mittee.
Just before the last State Senate ad
journed a committee was appointed to
Investigate the tax system of the State
and to report to the next Legislature any
suggestions for its improvement. The
committee was composed of Senators S.
<". Smith of Bakersfield, J. C. Sims of
Santa Rosa and E. R. Taylor of Alameda.
The first session of the committee was
held at the Lick House yesterday, the
members bringing business interests in
the city together so they could meet
There was little in the way of business
transacted, but plans for future work
were discussed and a general system of
work was agreed upon.
The committee will first send circular
letters to the various county Boards of
Supervisors, asking for information re
garding the cost of tax collection, also
statistics as to sources of revenue and
the proportions from each. Letters will
also be sent to the different States, in
quiring about different systems of taxa
tion. It was further decided to invite
communications from any one interested
in the subject, and any suggestions so
made will receive the careful attention of
the committee. These communications
may be sent to any member of the com
mitteeâSenator Smith at Bakersfield. Sen-
Sims at Santa Rosa or Senator Taylor at
Alameda.
The aim of the committee was stated
very broadly in the resolution providing
for its appointment. The resolution was
presented by Senator Smith, and his in
tention was to investigate and report; but
before the next sf-ssion the committee will
probably hnve gone further than that and
will have pr^par^d the form of a bill to
revise the tax system, or at least a re
vision and codification of the present tax
laws.
SAN JOSE NORMAL.
Important Meeting of the Directors
Monday.
The new board of directors of the San
Jose Normal School met in this city on
Monday and organized. Dr. Brown of
San Jose was elected chairman for one
year. A temporary executive committee
was appointed, consisting of Directors
Brown. Swiggert and Leavitt. Judge
Short of Fresno and State Superintendent
Kirk were elected members of the joint
board.
The election of the president of the San
Jose school was deferred to June 2. at
which time the hoard will meet in San
Jose. The candidates fur president are
the incumbent (Professor Randall t and
Professor C. \V. Cbilds. Professor Mc-
Naughton of Arizona has also filed an
application for the position. There has
been considerable friction at the school
for the past two years, and the meeting
of the board is being regarded with keen
Interest.
Professor Childs was president of the
school when Governor Budd took office
and was ousted by the Democratic board
of directors. It is regarded as possible
that he will be elected. Should Professor
Childs be again made president it may
lead to many changes in the teachers.
Under the rules the president nominates
all the teachers and the directors elect
them. On June 2 the board will also ap
portion the patronage of the clerks, jani
tors and general keepers.
Stole a Bill of Lading.
Moritz Politzer, nephew of Alexander
Politzer, liquor merchant. 503 California
street, swore to a complaint in Acting
Police Judge Groezinger's court yesterday
for the arrest of George Wollenschlager,
who kept a saloon at Butchertown, on the
charge of grand larceny. Wollenschlager
ordered from Politzer two barrels of
whisky that were shipped from the East.
When they arrived Politzer took the bill
k>f lading to Wollenschlager to collect the
amount due. $90, and he alleges that Wol
lenschlager grabbed him by the throat
and forcibly took the bill or lading from
him.
Have Leave to Mortgage.
\ Judge Daingerfield yesterday granted
leave to the board of trustees of the
Emanuel Baptist Church, the scene of the
Durrant murders, to mortgage the realty
! fr>r the Bum of $9000. A larger mortgage
heretofore existed, but it was reduced to
the present figure, the leave to mortgage
for $9000 being obtained to pay off a sub
sequent claim and to improve the church
proper.

THE CHARTER CLUB.
THE meeting at Metropolitan Temple on Mon
day night to organize a civic body for the pur
pose of influencing municipal administration in
the interest of property and industries was a very sig
nificant gathering. The 5! ! >y the chairman de
scribed the decline in values in San Francisco during
last decade, putting it at nearly half a billion dol
lars. The statement is sufficiently startling to stir to
activity the peoplt re injured by that mighty
shrinkage. The chairman presented the case by a
sentence that strikes the reader with all the interest
of novelty when he said the purpose of the meeting
was: "If possible, to determine some means by which
to prevent further loss to the owners of our city."
"The owners of the city." It would appear on the
surface that the city is owned by the politicians and
office-holders. They are in authority. They take the
taxes and spend them, and the property-owners run
hither and yon pleading for mercy in their assess
ments, looking in despair upon a rising taxable rate
dii a constantly falling actual value, until there is a
feeling abroad that they are the tenants of the prop
on and the office-holders are the landlords.
So it strikes one as novel when the property
owners are referred to truthfully as "the owners of
the city." It is their property. That property has
fallen in value for ten years at the rate of fifty mil
lions a year, and its owners have at last concluded to
take matters into their own hands and find out why
their property, the city, has lost value at such a
rapid rate.
The gods stand off while the man crie6 for help to
pull his wagon out of the mire; but when he puts
hiÂ« own shoulder to the wheel and says nothing, but
lifts with all his might, then all Olympus runs to his
tance.
The owners of this city have been invoking all the
powers of earth and air and .vater to come to their
assistance and lift the value of their property out of its
decline. But now they have taken hold themselves,
and it their expressed purpose is carried out politics
will no longer hold a club over them, but they will
hold a club over politics and harness it to their policy
for the municipal, commercial and industrial regenera
tion of San Francisco.
This city wants more business and the greater popu
lation that it will bring. Half million clubs are use
less things if they propose to merely bring immigra
tion without bringing also the business that must sup
port it. It is not only through municipal administra
tion that lost values are to be recovered, but through
individual and organized enterprise in business.
San Francisco has not had enough of this enter
prise. Business plants that shou.d have come here
have gone elsewhere. For the sake of that prime
economy in transportation, the bringing of ship and
car together, commerce has gone up the bay to Port
Costa, and much of it farther away to Puget Sound
ports, because ship and car are kept apart in this
city, which has the best facilities in the world for
bringing them together. European cities have spent
millions of dollars to secure that facility which is put
here by nature and without cost.
The Central Republican Charter Club will find
nniple scope for its work in the interest of the own
ers of this city, and it must first convince them that
they nre the owners, and that their interests are mu
tual nnd common. How would the affairs of a stock
company flourish if its owner-, the stockholders,
should consider themselves Republicans or Demo
crats in a sense superior to their character as owners
of the property? How would things go if on election
day a competent ticket for directors was opposed by
a ticket of incompetents who would be elected be
cause their political partisans were in a majority in
â¢' â  company? The owners of this city are the own
'â  of it- property. Their interests are common and
tv separable. What hurts one hurts all. Let them re
metnber this and determine to stand together as the
owners of a common interest, and municipal adminis
tration will become a business affair in which the in
terests of the owners of the city will be considered
and all the people will be helped by it.
Thomas Reed of Maine may not be the most lov
able man in the world, but there is no disputing his
caliber. Any budding statesman with designs on the
retiring Speaker's chair would do well to furnish him
self with guy ropes to keep from slithering around on
its highly polished surface.
The Prince of Wales, the dispatches announce,
affects a pronounced liking to hear American "coon"
songs whistled. Is it possible that his Royal High
ness contemplates the introduction of the cake walk
into the aristocratic and exclusive circles in which
he moves?
THE MARKET STREET RAILROAD
INIQUITY.
WHEN the cars of the Market street combina
tion straddle the crossings of the streets or
pass them at a high rate of speed, ignoring the
motions of waiting customers, there are a few gen
j tlcmen in San Francisco who always take of! their
hats to indicate their appreciation of Mr. Hunting
ton's absolute rule and of the fact that pedestrians
have no rights that the Market Street Railway Com
' i any is bound to respect.
It had been supposed that, owing to legal obstacles,
1 the attempted grab by this arrogant monopoly of
whole sections of the city so as to reduce almost the
I entire territory north and much of it south of Mar
' ket street to its absolute control had been tem
porarily abandoned. But, at the meeting of the
Board of Supervisors on Monday last, the community
was undeceived, for all the former demands were re
\ived and extended, with the recorded sanction of
seven members of the board. Nothing but a technical
blunder, so far as can be seen,, prevented the entire
! grab as amended from reaching the first legal stage on
j the road to success.
In the editorial columns of The Call on the 7th and
1 Bth of May the comprehensiveness and the rascality of
j this audacious invasion of public right were clearly
1 exposed. It was not then certain, however, that a
majority of the Street Committee of the Board of Su
pervisors were ready, in private session, to accept any
proposal the Market Street Railway Company had to
offer, in order to thwart the operation of the new
charter, or that a majority of the board itself were
eager to act as ratifying agents of the monopoly.
This flaunting contempt of the pablic will and of the
public interests was reserved for the proceedings of
Monday.
The details of this unprecedented scheme of plun
der have now been perfected. It includes the re
mainder of Sansome street north of Pacific street, a
block on Battery street between Sacramento and Cali
, fotnia streets, a block on Stockton street between
I Pacific street and Broadway, and two approaches to
the city and county line, one terminating on Milliken
-treet and the other on Forty-ninth avenue, near the
Cliff House. All these franchises are exacted for fifty
; years, or double the time allowed by the new charter.
The present Geary street franchise, which would ex
pire November 6. 1903, it is proposed to surrender on
j condition that a new and coextensive franchise, cov
ering electricity and all other motive powers, be
granted. The new franchises on Taylor street be
tween Geary and Post streets and along Grant avenue
I from its intersection with Geary street to Bush street
â  and down Bush street to Sansome street are of course
demanded. The use of electricity and of other im
provements in motive power is also required for the
whole system. The new charter not only limits the
duration of street railroad franchises to one-half the
period now permitted, but explicitly declares that no
such franchise shall be granted unless the percentage
ci gross receipts offered is at least 3 per cent for the
first five years, 4 per cent for the next ten and 5 per
I cent for the last ten years. The drafted resolutions now
1 presented by the Market Street Railway Company
relieve the corporation from the payment of any per
centage for five years. The statute of March 13. 1897,
: only requires 3 per cent to be contributed for the last
forty-five years of the franchise. The new charter,
furthermore, enables the municipality "to fix. establish
1 and reduce the fares and charges for transporting pas
sengers and goods." and contains other stringent
â  provisions for the protection of the public which are
i not embodied in the existing laws.
This is the most atrocious and unspeakably dishon
est plan to destroy possible competition and to
firmly intrench a railroad despotism in our midst of
which there is any record. The bulkhead bills of 1859
and 1800, which sought to place the entire water front
in the hands of French capitalists, were supposed at -
the time to have no precedent for infamy and for :
audacity. But in 1899 the Market Street Railway
Company has easily surpassed that wholesale scheme
of confiscation which the Supervisors of forty years
ago repudiated, and of the triumph of which there
was never any danger while an honest Governor pos
sessed the veto power. Now, in the full glare of pub
licity, with the facts and with the law thoroughly ven
tilated and understood, it appears that all Mr. Hun
tington has to do is to signify his pleasure to seven
members of the board and his intricate robbery is ap
| proved, before even its details could have been read.
As the new charter goes into effect on January 1,
1900. and as the existing franchise of the Geary Street
Railway Company has more than four years to run. it
i e impossible for any part of the vile project which
has been thus impudently uncovered, and especially
! lor so much of it as relates to Geary street, to suc
ceed without manifest corruption. Cranks and
fanatics are often unjust to public officers, but this is
a proposition that the most tolerant citizen in San
Francisco cannot fail to indorse. To give franchises
for half a century, and to deprive the city of not less
than 3 per cent on gross receipts for five years, and
of an increasing proportion for forty-five years
I longer, when, by waiting for seven months, the new
I law that the people and the Legislature have ratified
i can be enforced, is transparently iniquitous and in-
I capable of explanation. And these are only isolated
i specifications of the enormous injury that this com
munity is expected to endure. The monopoly could
afford to pay a million dollars to consummate the
steal.
If the opinion of the City and County Attorney
upon the questions arising from this latest railroad
fraud has been delivered there has been no publication
of the fact. But appearances indicate that neither
law, evidence nor our metropolitan necessities, which
are increasing year by year, are to receive the slight
i est consideration at the hands of Mr. Huntington or
j of a bare majority of the Board of Supervisors.
The patience of the property-holding citizens of
San Francisco has been frequently and severely taxed,
! but this last raid upon the municipality transcends all
former experience.
THE CONTEST FOR THE SPEAK
ERSHIP
WHILE Tom Reed has not yet given the pub
lic a formal notice of his intention to retire
from political life, and has not resigned his
seat in Congress, it is abundantly evident that he has
given his friends notice that he will not be a candi
date for the of this Congress. That
much is demonstrated by the activity of the canvass
now going on for the office.
The number of candidates is about as large as that
of States having strong Republican delegations in
Congress, for while there are some such States hav
ing no candidate, there are several which have two or
more, and the average holds good. It is gratifying to
note that thus far hardly any candidate has been put
forward upon sectional grounds, though of course
such considerations are sure to come later. For the
present the one desire of the party appears to be that
of choosing the best man for the place.
Numerous as are the aspirants, only a few of
them have any strong following at this time. Among
these are three New York men, Sherman, Payne and
THE SAN FRANCISCO , CALL, WEDNESDAY, MAY 24. 1899.
Ray. The first two are men of long experience and
represent the veterans of the party, while Ray is a
young warhorse and hopes to win by dash and vigor
rather than upon his record.
Illinois has a veteran candidate in the well-known
"Joe" Cannon, who as chairman of the Committee on
Appropriations has rendered good service to his party
1 and proven himself an able debater. The same State
has also a young man candidate in Mr. Hopkins, who,
however, has some claims as a veteran also, since he
has served in eight Congresses. He isinow making
a more active canvass than any other candidate and
has already gone to Washington to catch any stray
Representative who may be there.
Ohio has a candidate in Mr. Grosvenor, who dur
ing the last Congress was looked upon as the spokes
man of the administration and whose skill as a de
bater and as a parliamentarian is well known. lowa
presents for the office General David Bremer Hender
son, a veteran of Congress and of the Civil War â a
man not only of strong but of picturesque character
and much given to the use of language that requires
explanation. For example, he is now being charged
with having said that American hams for export
ought to be wrapped in the stars and stripes, and
his enemies hold he has more reverence for the lowa
ham than the banner of his country, despite his ser
vice under h in war.
It is probable the contest may be prolonged to such
a point that a dark horse will have a chance to win.
Should that prove so. the Pacific Coast should cer
tainly have a candidate to put forward, not on sec
tional grounds, but upon the broad principle that it
is time for the nation to recognize the services of
Pacific Coast men. Mr. Loud has risen steadily in j
the estimation of the House and of the country dur- j
ing his service in Congress and would be a good man
to support should there be any chance for a man
from the coast.
THE STRAW BOND INVESTIGATION.
I-UJROM every point of view the steps taken by the
Grand Jury to investigate the straw bond
frauds alleged to have been practiced by Police
Court Clerk Kelly are gratifying, and it is to be
hoped the inquiry will be eventually extended to
cover the whole range of such offenses.
The straw bond evil is one which has long been
complained of in every important city in the United
States. It is an outgrowth of the shyster element
which has been permitted to attach itself to the bar,
and which by its own corruption has corrupted the
practices of the courts themselves and led to the de
velopment of a hundred forms of trickery, cunning
and fraud to defeat the law.
Every now and then there occurs some flagrant in
stance of such corruption to arouse public indigna
tion, and then there is an investigation, which for a
time renders the tricksters more cautious in their
work. Rarely, however, does it happen in any city
that wholesale convictions of offenders take place
and adequate punishment is meted out. Therefore
any earnest effort to accomplish -nich results is always
a matter for public congratulation.
From the reports given out of the results of the in
vestigation thus far, there appears good prospect that
ample evidence will be obtained in the investigations
now undertaken by the Grand Jury to obtain at least
one conviction. Kelly seems to have carried on the
practice of issuing straw bonds with such boldness
that his offenses can be easily proven even under the
restrictions placed upon legal testimony.
It is stated that there have been submitted to the
Grand Jury over one hundred bonds accepted by
Kelly which bond experts have declared to be worth
less. Some of the saloon-keepers who signed bonds
which Kelly accepted are reported to have admitted
they signed them for political reasons, while others
assigned business reasons for their action. One of
the latter is quoted as having said: "In every case
where I went as surety on a bond I did so as a matter
of business. When a frequenter of my saloon had the
misfortune to be arrested I gladly consented to be
come one of his bondsmen rather than have him stay
in jail over night."
It appears, moreover, that in the course of the in
vestigation the Grand Jury has discovered evidence
that in many crises criminals have been released be
fore the bonds required by law were riled. Thus it is
said that a woman, a notorious pickpocket, >vas once
released on an order from Kelly five minutes after she
was arrested, while the required bonds were not fur
nished and placed on record in court until the fol
lowing day.
The people of San Francisco have seen so many
prosecutions based upon seemingly irresistible evi
dence come to nothing that they will not be sanguine
of conviction in this casr, notwithstanding the
strength of the testimony reported to have been given.
Still they will be well pleased if the Grand Jury con
tinue its efforts. Exposure is not as good as convic
tion, but it helps to hold rascality in check, and that
is something.
The people of the United States persist in insisting
that this is not a warlike nation. It may not be. but
any foreigrr statesman who will take the trouble to
glance at the programme of exercises for the coming
Memorial day will hardly be convinced. He will see
there represented the veterans of three wars â not in
cluding the Filipino affair â and will probably come to
the conclusion that your Uncle Sam is all right when
he is let alone and all fight when he is not.
The sugar industry has grown to such magnitude in
Hawaii that it has been found necessary to encroach
upon the lands formerly used for the pasturage of
cattle. As a consequence they are experiencing a
meat famine in Honolulu, which the merchants of
San Francisco will find profit in averting.
It is hardly necessary for Attorney General Ford to
deny the statement in a dispatch from Sacramento
that Governor Gage had sought his advice concerning
the legality of certain appointments. It is evident
from the many breaks made by the Governor that he
has sought advice from no one.
| / Brassey's Annual announces with great serious
! ness that the battle of Manila Bay conveyed only les
sons of an obvious character. The instruction was
certainly very plain to those Spaniards that were left
! after the engagement.
John McGoomis not only vindicated his own man
hood but he struck a blow for an absent man when
be landed on one Bermah Speck for disrespectful re
marks concerning Admiral Dewey.
i Czar Nicholas has notified the Baron de Staal that
he desires international arbitration even if it be com
pulsory. If that is not a distinctly Russian way of
j looking at a thing, what is it?
It is wired from Chicago, as an entirely novel
proposition, that gamblers in that city pay for police
protection. In what other way could they have se
cured it?
Sharkey says that Fitzsimmons can inflict terrific
punishment. Sharkey certainly ought to know, but
who advised him to be candid?
SAN FRANCISCO
SHOULD HAVE
A BIG EXHIBIT
Representation at the
State Fair.
APPEAL TO SUPERVISORS
THE BOARD OF AGRICULTURE
TAKES ACTION.
President A. B. Spreckels Gives Rea
f one Why the Metropolis Should
Make a Good Showing
This Year-
An urgent appeal has been made to the
Board of Supervisors by the State Board
of Agriculture, to secure proper represen
tation of the city's industries at the State
Fair to be held in Sacramento next fall.
The reason why such representation from
the State's metropolis should be made are
set forth at length and will no doubt re
ceive proper consideration at the hands
of the board. The appeal comes to the
board in the form of a communication
from A. B. Spreckels, president of the
State Board of Agriculture, and is aa
follows:
May 19, 1899.
To the Board of Supervisorsâ Gentlemen: At
a recent meeting ot the State Agricultural
Society It was determined to communicate
with the Boards of Supervisors of the various
counties and tv request them to have prepared
an exhibit of the agricultural products and
resources of their respective counties.
The directors desire to give the State Fair
a State character and to make it as broadly
useful to every section of the State as pos
sible; to make It a medium for the supplying
of agricultural and industrial Information, the
promotion of Internal developments and the
encouragement of desirable Immigration.
The usual attendance at a State Fair Is very
large, and a great number of persons from
abroad who are contemplating locating or in
vesting In this State, or who might be in
duced to locate or invest here, come to the
State FRir to view the products of our State
and to determine her agricultural and indus
trial possibilities. To the end that our great
variety ot products should be given proper
display and that accurate and reliable evi
dence may be given of the special products
of each section of the Staff, the directors de
sire that an exhibit of the products of each
county be prepared by the Board of Super
visors of eacfi county for exhibition at the
appioacning State Ifair.
A separate of the Exhibition Pa
vilion will be set apart for these county ex
hibits, which will t.e set up by the society
without expense, and a proper and qualified
attendant will be put in charge who will pre
sent and explain in detail to travelers and
the general public. If the county, or any
Board of Trade, Immigration Society or Cham
ber of Commerce organized within It desire
to supply any literature or printed matter for
distribution In connection with this display,
it will be so distributed by the person in
charge. If the county or any county organ
ization should desire to employ a special at
tendant for its exhibit no objection will be
made.
As to the character and extent of the county
exhibits, the boar.l desires that this should
be left entirely to the discretion of the county
authorities making the exhibit. The exhibit
should contain samples or displays of the
leading and most valuable products of the re
spective counties. This could be done with
such simplicity or elaboration as the re
sources of' the board necessitate. It can be
made very .small and inexpensive or It may
be made much more extensive and costly. In
your county, for example, your people are
largely Interested in fruit, green, dried and
preserved; raisins, wines, wheat, barley, corn,
hay, alfalfa, hops, dairy produce, etc. Sam
ples of these products, of such size and ex
tent, mounted or prepared in such manner as
your taste and Judgment dictate, would form
a sufficient representation of your agricultural
wealth.
These exhibits, when properly prepared, will
be or primary and principal importance to
the county making them, but when all pre
pared will constitute an industrial and agri
cultural man of California and will be of
such general benefit that It is to be hoped
that every county will, with proper public
spirit, contribute its share.
ThÂ« State Board of Agriculture very much
defiirer that you should give this" matter such
attention as It requires, and fhat you will
do so at a sufficiently early day to provide
for the collection of all the different articlf-s
in proper season. The cost of the collection
by you will be very small Indeed. You can
make it as small as the resources at your
command necessitate. The society will at
tend to Its mounting and display and will
provide for Its explanation and presentation
to the greatest possible number of spectators.
Hoping that your board will take this mat
ter up at your earliest convenience and notify
the society of your favorable action. I am.
Yours respectfully. A. B. SPRECKELS.
President.
PETER J. SHIELDS,
Secretary.

An Interesting Itinerary That In
cludes a Glimpse of Every Branch
of Agriculture Represented
in California.
The work of preparing a reception to
the Association of American Agricultural
Colleges, which will meet here early in
July, was well started yesterday in the
rooms of the Chamber of Commer*.
Notices had been sent to all thejmem
bere to attend a meeting to discuss plans
of procedure, and before it closed a well
defined idea had been formulated for the
two committees that were appointed.
Mayor Phelan presided and Professor
Jaffa, assistant professor at the Univer
sity of California, explained the purpose
of the meeting. He showed what an ad
vertisement for California the convention
would be if the members of it were only
given an opportunity to study conditions
of agriculture in this State. He said there
had been planned a trip of perhaps a
thousand miles, which would lake in
Sonoma, Napa, Sacramento, Santa Clara
and San JoaQuin valleys. The railroad
had offered a rate of $1 a mile for the
special, and had offered to stop the train
anywhere where it would not interfere
with general traffic. Mr. Foster of the
San Francisco and North Pacific Rail
road had also made an offer of transpor
tation. He would give a special for the
Sonoma Valley.
Professor Jaffa estimated that there
would at least 150 in the party, and the
trips planned would take eight or nine
days. Mr. Mills supplemented Professor
Jaffa's remarks with a few suggestions
as to the itinerary. His idea was to take
the visitors to the great centers of in
dustry, such as Chico for harvesting,
Napa' for deciduous fruits, Salinas for
beet sugar, Orovllle for citrus fruits, Ba
i kersfield for irrigation and Marin for
dairying. It was understood Los Angeles
and the south would look after the trav
elers south of Tehachapi.
A. C. Darby, president of the Board of
! Trade of San Jose, was on hand to offer
the co-operation of San Jose in any plan
for entertainment.
