GAS COMPANY
WILL APPEAL
TO THE COURTS
Afraid Its Bills Will
Not Be Paid.
LIGHTS MAY BE SHUT OFF
DESPERATE MEASURES NOW ON
THE PROGRAMME.
The Corporation Would Restrain the
Board of Supervisors From Us
ing Money in the Street
Light Fund.
The action taken by the Board of Su
pervisors Monday in again deferring final
authorization for the payment of the bills
of the San Francisco Gas and Electric
Company bids fair to force the hand of
the corporation because of the fact that
there is a strong probability that there
will be an attempt to dispose of the
money in the fund used for the payment
of the city's light bills before the present
â contest between the minority and the ma
jority in the board is adjusted. Should
this be done the company will be left with
unpaid light bills amounting in round fig
ures to $185,000 on its hands at the end of
the fiscal year. As the debts contracted
in one fiscal year cannot be paid out of
the funds of another year the corporation
would be left without recourse and the
sum named would represent a net loss.
Finding themselves face to face with
this serious proposition the officials inti
mated yesterday that they would have
recourse to one or both of two alterna
tives. One is that they will apply to the
courts for an injunction to restrain the
Board of Supervisors from transferring
money from the street light fund to any
other fund or for any other purpose than
that of paying the city's light bills. The
other is that they will shut off all of the
city's gas and electric lights and leave
the community in darkness.
This latter course would have the effect
of bringing about an action that would
result in an order of court directing the
gas company to continue to serve the city
with gas and electric light pending a legal
settlement of the controversy. Pending
such determination through legal chan
nels the bills of the company would prob
ably be paid by order of court, such or
ders being payable out of any funds in
the city treasury. In this manner the
corporation would at least insure to itself
the payment of its light bills for the bal
ance of the fiscal year. It is not believed,
however, that this course will be adopted
for the reason that it would tend to preju
dice public opinion against the gas com
pany.
It is certain that the gas company will
take such legal steps as may be deemed
recessary to prevent the Board of Super
visors from disposing of the money in
the street light fund. If this is done the
authorizations for the payment of the
company's bills for November. December,
January. February and March will be
passed and they will then be vetoed by
the Mayor. This will give the corpora
tion the opportunity to apply to the courts
for the fixing of The legal rate to be paid
for gas. ________^____

Burglary in the City Hall.
The office of Prosecuting Attorney Car
penter In the City Hall was entered by a
burglar Sunday night and an overcoat,
book and a pair of rubbers belonging to
Dr. Sarsfield, the German Interpreter,
were stolen. The burglar effected an en
trance by forcing open the window that
opens on the esplanade running around
the central court. It is supposed it is the
same burglar that broke into the Sheriff's
office.

talist.
Rulofson recited in detail the unhappy
life he led with his father and the rea
sons that prompted him to leave home
and tiik>- passage on a sailing
which ultimately led him to mi <t his ul
k-sed adopted father, <"aptain Hall. The
witness testified that after being prom
ised "a good whipping." he threw hla
clothes 'out of the window of the room
In which he was confined and jumped out
after them. He went to the home of a
friend and then went to yea. Subsequent
ly he met Captain Hall, and through an
agreement between that person and his
r he was adopted by the former.
Captain Hall died and left a will in which.
for some reason. Kulofson was not men
; tinned, so he determined to bring the
suit. After giving his testimony the wit
was excused and the cn^e went over
till this morning, at which time the cross
examination will commence.
FIREMEN RESCUE TWO
MEN FROM GAS FUMES
FATHER AND SON NEARLY
ASPHYXIATED.
Mrs. Smith Awakens to Find the
House Filled With the Fluid
and Her Family Un
conscious.
Firemen who proved to be equal to
other emergencies than those of fire res-
I cued C. H. Smith and A. L. Smith, who
â  were nearly overcome by escaping gas
at 1453 Valencia street, early yesterday
morning. Both men were fast becoming
victims of the deadly fumes when the fire
fighters, attracted by the cries of a
"frightened wife and mother, rushed from
the house of engine 13, directly opposite,
and dragging the victims to , the street
restored them to consciousness.
A. L. Smith, who is well advanced in
: years, occupied a room connected with
the back apartments of the house. His
son, C. H. Smith, slept in an adjoining
1 room. About 5 o'clock yesterday morning
Mrs. Smith awoke, nearly stifled by gas. ;
; She attempted to awaken her husband, i
but he could not. move. Entering her '
son's apartments she discovered that a
jet in the center of the room was leaking
and tried to awaken him. He partially !
regained his senses, but soon relapsed
Into a comatose state. Then she rushed
to the front porch, where she cried for
help. .â -â ?. -â â 'â -â '
James Rlley, James O Connor, J. Mc-
Quade and John Pendergast, all firemen,
1 heard her cries and went to her assist
ance. When they entered the room they
could hardly endure the gas-laden atmos
phere. They. threw open the windows and
pulled the men outside. Both were un- |
conscious, and it required some effort to
resuscitate them. After dispatching Of
-1 fleer Potter for Dr. Coffey at 11-12 Valen
cia street the firemen worked hard and j
soon had the men out of danger. When |
the physician arrived hp administered i
restoratives and his patients were able to |
be about last evening. .

HAS FOUND A FRIEND.
Colonel Henry Moore, the Old Miner
Arrested for Vagrancy, Gets
His Freedom.
Colonel Henry Moore, miner and ex
attache of the Shah of Persia, has found
a friend, and yesterday Judge Mogan. af
ter admonishing him not to beg on Mont
gomery street any more, allowed him to
go, with the promise that if he again
found himself in absolute want he would
give him an order to admit him to the
Almshouse.
Yesterday morning the Judge received
a letter from Frank Van Tassel. 368 Elev
enth street, who said he had been a miner
in Western Australia, and added: "Please
send Colonel Moore to me and I will as
sist him, as I know he is telling the
truth." The old miner got Van Tassel' 3
address and said he would call upon him.
He seemed delighted to find that his story
of his experiences in Coolgardle. Western
Australia, had been corroborated.

Point for Mary M. Morrisey,
Judge Murasky yesterday overruled the
demurrer of the Board of Education to
the complaint of Mary M. Morrisey, who
seekÃ to be reinstated to her position as
teacher in thÂ© John Swett Grammar
School. In its demurrer the board al
leged that it had arbitrarily closed the
school in question, but the court held that
the board could not arbitrarily put an end
to the existence of a school; it must have
cause. The demurrer was therefore over
ruled and the board given ten days to
answer.
THE EMBANKMENT
THROUGH THE MARSH
STATEMENT OF GENERAL MAN
AGER KRUTTSCHNITT.
The Condition of the Track on the
Alaxneda Marsh in No Respect
Dangerous.
CARD TO THE PUBLIC.
The settling of the embankment of
the South Pacific Coast road in the
marsh on the south shore of San Lean
dro Bay, to which considerable atten
tion has been given of late by the press,
does not introduce any element of dan
ger in the operation of trains, and, but
for some slight delay that was experi
enced on two or three occasions, would
no doubt have passed without notice.
In throwing up the embankment the
i builders of the road excavated ditches
i In the marsh, leaving little or no space
between them and the toe of the slope
ito support the bank. The action of the
1 tides in running through these side
ditches has prevented them from filling
i up, as would otherwise have been the
case, and the settling that has recently
taken place was no doubt immediately
caused by the recent ballasting of the
| piece of track in question, increasing
somewhat the height and weight of the
embankment.
On the first signs of trouble watch
men were placed on duty and never at
any time was there the slightest danger
of trains running into the sink. When
i ever settling took place temporary re
! pairs were made and the work now in
I progress is sufficiently well advanced to
warrant the expectation that the
trouble at that point will be perma
nently remedied. The remedy Is a very
; simple one. The Bide or borrow ditches
will be damned to prevent the forward
and backward rush of tide waters, after
which they will be tilled up with stone
and other heavy material to balance
the weight of the embankment and pre
vent subsidence. This remedy, which
has proven entirely successful in num
bers of similar cases of settling on
other parts of the company's lines, will
be applied wherever there are signs of
possible settlement, and this statement
is made for the purpose of assuring the
I public, our patrons, who are using the
i line in question, that they run no risk
of accident. No train is permitted to
cross the repaired track without sig
nals from the watchman, and, should
a subsidence, through remote possibil
ity, take place under a passing train,
the speed is so slow that accident would
be impossible.
The trouble in question is by no
j means unusual. It has been experi-
I enced in the marshes between Benicia
and Suisun ami between Port Costa and
Antioch, in this State; in the marshes
I bordering on the Atchafalaya River,
! nnd in the trembling prairies or
i marshes bordering on Bayou dcs Alle
i mands in Louisiana. In most of these
cases, whore the sinks were far more
serious and extensive than the one in
the Alameda marsh, the same method
of treatment was applied. In no case
did any accident happen, although the
I train movements were very much
heavier than on the South Pacific Coast
Railway. J. KRUTTBCHNITT.
Fourth Vice President and General
Manager, Southern Pacific Company.
I San Francisco, Cal., May 23, 1899. I

T. J. Parsons of the Del Monte Milling
Company swore to a complaint in Acting
Police Judge Groezinger's court yesterday
for the arrest of rharles F. Deane, an
ex-clerk with the company, on the charge

leged that on March 30 Deane collected a

Haquette, a saloon-keeper at 16 Post
! street, and got drunk on the proceeds,
' which led to his discharge. The com
pany's collector went to Ilaquette on May
17 to collect the bill and ascertained that
Deane had collected it. Deane was called
upon at his residence, and said the money
I had been stolen from him.

DRIED AND CANNED FRUITS IN NEW
?ORK.
The New York Commercial says
-Reports from the coast regarding prunes are
mere or less unsatisfactory. Nothing definite
Is known yet, but it is certain that a majority
of the interests involved Incline to the opinion
that the crop will be more or less short. Sonoma
County an.l the Santa Clara valley both report
nome shortage, but what the percentage is it is
Impossible to say. The spot market is firm, but
prices are unchanged. Probably medium sizes
meet most Inquiry, but lots ordered are chiefly
Fmall and go to retail dealers or country dis
tributors.
"Raisins are firm and three-crown are a frac
tion higher, the demand from seeders being un
usually large. Reports from the coast have
caused no change In the market, except to In
crease the firmness. Holders are reluctant sell
ers, and business is limited to small lots for
Immediate consumption.
"Peaches are steady, with upward tendency.
The present outlook favors thÂ» assumption that
the proportion available for drying will be
small, and that prices are likely to rule high
In consequence.
"Apricots are steady, but the range is above
most buyers' Ideas, and little business results
from the inquiry. With a promised crop of per
haps 880 cars there will probably be a more
active market than has ruled this {rear.
"Canned Goodsâ The feature of the market is
â the large sale of California canned products for
future delivery, at opening prices, announced
exclusively in the Commercial last week. Some
packers are reported entirely sold out. an.i oth
ers have advanced prices on cherries, apricots
and lemon cling peaches. This action will hard
ly restrict the sale, the demand at present be
ing enormous.
"On the spot thero Is little change. All sup
plies are held firm because of the shortage of
all varieties At no time in the past ten years
has the supply of canned goixls been as small
as it is now. and holders are firm In their
views because of thai There is a cessation In
the demand for vegetables, but all supplies nr<
held firmly up to market prices, and traae,
though limited, is steady."

the high pr demanded. Exports for the first i

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. May 23.â Neglect of the Stock !
Exchange by the outside public continued to j
he thÂ« marked feature of the trading, and, i
as Is usual In a narrow market, prices drifted
uneasily up and down, following the erratic i
movements of special stocks. Dealings were
evidently almost wholly confined to the pro
fessional element, who seek profits on the
day* turn in prices. As this class of dealers
watch pretty closely for what other people
are doing and proceed to do likewise, the net .
effect in a day's business, or taking one day â 
with another, is not large. Yesterday's late
upward movement was followed by a realiz
ing this morning and later by a sharp ad- j
vance, which was practically wiped out be- j
fore the close. Sugar was the center of In- j
terest in the market and rebounded quite j
â trongly from the early pressure, together
with the affiliated glucose stocks.
But realizing took off all the early gain and
left it at the last five points below the high- I
eft and near the low point of the day. The j
drop in Sugar was accompanied by a strong ;
rally In the New York municipal stocks. Con
solidated Gas. Manhattan, Brooklyn Transit
and Metropolitan rising from 2 to 4 points.
This concurrent movement in the leading spe
cialties gave quite an appearance of. strength
to the market for a time, and their stocks
gained in the upward movement. The weak
ness in wheat, especially the reported selling
from the Southwest, helped this movement, j
but the pervlstent reports of Insect damage |
and news from abroad that American wheat
was practicably unsalable at present prices |
helped the late break in Grangers and South
westerns. There was a growth of uneasiness j
also over the persistent hardening tendency
of foreign exchange. The actual rate for de
mand sterling touched $4 \l\ to-day, which
Is near the gold export point.
The continuing ease in money suggests that
the method employed to finance the Philip- :
pine payment to Spain has forced something j
of a redundancy in the local money market i
resulting from the rather artificial stiffness |
of exchange rates Exchange on Merlin also j
hardened a fraction to-day, although that
money center was reported to be buying I
American stocks in London.
The newly floated Industrial stocks contin
ued rather conspicuous by weakness. Ana
conda broke 2 points, and In the outside mar
ket Amalgamated Copper, the payment of
subscriptions for which was completed yester
day, fell to par, which Is the subscription
price. The closing on the Exchange was easy
In tone, though rather dull, and showed net â 
losses In nil but a few cases.
There was a good demand for some of the '
reorganization 4 per cent railroad bonds, the
Union Pacific and Northern Pacific 4s both l
touching record prices. Total sales, par value,
13.744,000.
United States Government bonds unchanged
The total sales of stock to-day amounted to
408.628 shares. Including: American Steel and â

P200; Anaconda. 4500; Ann Arbor preferred,
1100; Atchison preferred. 500; Brooklyn j

THE STOCK MARKET.
Securities were fairly active on the morning
session. Oceanic sold down to $88 75, Onomea
Plantation to $43 75 and Paauhau to $41 75.
Other changes were insignificant.
Business was quiet in the afternoon. Oceanic
was still weaker at $SS as the low figure.
.Mining: stocks were firmer, especially on the
afternoon call, and sales were larger in several
mines. The telegram from the pump said: "We
are keeping the water down near the 1950 level
by running the second elevator on half time."
The Con. Cal. & Va. assessment of 25c per
share Is delinquent in office to-day, and the
Potosl assessment of 10c per share will be de
linquent in the company's office to-morrow.
The Sherman Gold and Sliver Mining- Com
pany of Pyramid mining district, Washoe
County, Nev., has levied an assessment of one
tenth of lc per share, delinquent June 20.
The following shipments of ore were made
from the mines of Eureka district. New. by
rail for the week ending May 1!). Bullwhacker
mine, 66,620 pounds; Diamond, 58,200; Eureka
Con.. 73,410: Hamburg, 92.760; Jackson, 67,550;
Oriental and Belmont, 4200.
â  The Standard Consolidated Mining Company
of Bodle will pay a dividend of 10c per share
to-day.
The annual meeting of the Crown Point Min
ing Company has been called for June 5.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographio
Office located In the Merchants Exchange, is
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
"Tfavigators^are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete sata of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can alwayi be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation : and all matter*
of Interest to ocean commerce. Ferry
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped Â«
noon. 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Nat ai
Observatory, Mare Island. Cal. Âââ-. â 
A notice stating whether the ball was drppr -1
on time or giving the error, if any. is published
in the morning papers the following day.
C. G. CALKINS,
Lieutenant. U. S. N . In charge.
SUN, MOON AND TIDE.
United States Coast and Geodetic Survey-
Times and Heights of High and Low
Waters at Fort Point, entrance to San
Francisco Bay. Published by official au
thority of the Superintendent.
NOTEâ The high and low waters occur at
the city front (Mission-street wharf) about
twenty-five minutes later than at Fort Point;
the height of tide is the same at both places.
TUESDAY. MAY 24.
NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
thÂ» early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide, of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives thÂ»
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on thÂ»
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted fro~^^
the depth given by the charts. The plane ?.'f
reference is the mean of the lnwer low waters.

The Oceanic Steamship Company's Aus
tralia arrived from Honolulu yesterday
with over 150 passengers. The run was
made in the fast time of 6 days 1 hour and
21 minutes and was uneventful, with the
exception of the. shooting of Gelespie, a
waiter, by Wade, the cook, and the shoot
ing of the latter by Chief of Police Evans
of Honolulu.
The hospital ship Solace was in port at
Honolulu when the Australia left. She
arrived there on May 15, and was to coal
as quickly as possible and proceed to San
Francisco. She has a large number of
invalids and time-expired soldiers aboard,
and also Hrigadier General Charles King.
The general was to have come up from
Manila on the Sheridan, but changed his
I mind, thinking: the Solace would get here
first. Lieutenants Minett and Gorgas of
1 the Bennington are also passengers on
: the Solace.

unkn! $24 the thousand feet. On that oc

19 reins per foot for the same article. It
did not take the Commissioners long to
decide upon what to do. All the bids
were rejected.

The ship Eclipse left Port Gajnble on
December 25 for Algoa Bay with 1465.500
feet of lumber, valued at $10.564. She ar
rived at her destination on March 26. but
has not yet completed discharging. <m
April 25 a terrific storm came up and the
ship's cables parted. As she was being
driven ashore three tugs proffered their
assistance and agreed to tow the Eclipse
to a safe anchorage for $1500. rapiain
Peterson accepted the offer and now the
insurance companies will have to charge
that amount to profit and loss.
David Van Iderstine. a collector for
the San Francisco Lumber Company, fell
down in an apoplectic fit at the corner of
Montgomery and Po^t streets yesterday.
He was removed to the Harbor Hospit il
and attended by Dr. yon der Leith. He is
a very sick man and may not recover.
Sailors who ship here for a voyage to
Australia must be able to read and write
or rise they will not be allowed to land
in the colonies. The San Francisco ship
Oarrolton took three natives in her crew
and the captain had to put up a $l.VÂ»0 bond
for them. They escaped, but luckily proof
was forthcoming that they had shipped^ on
another vessel, so the money whs not for
feited. This state of affairs was hrought
about by a recent act of the Australian
Parliament.

Soldiers on a Rampage.
Ralph P. Avery and L,endorf Stevens;
two soldiers from Kansas, got drunk
Monday night and because M. J. Collins,
saloon keeper, 2816 Greenwich street, re
fused to give them liquor they threw
rocks at the bar and broke five bottles
Of liquor. They were arrested for mali

ston he dismissed the case and advised

THREATENED TO
SHOOT IF NOT
GIVEN A LOIN
Gripman Mott Very
Much in Love.
MISS SCHOMBERG'S PERIL
A STRUGGLE FOR LIFE IN A
LAWYERS OFFICE.
The Lady's Attorney, Hugh S. Aid
rich, Finds Many Mementoes
of the Contest in His
Waiting Room.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
Broadway, May 23.
"Murder! Murder! Police! Police!"
m the oilice of At-
THE STRUGGLE FOR THE PISTOL.
torney Hugh 6. Aldrlch In the Blake &
Moffltt block to-day rapidly drew a crowd
into the corridors. By the time Professor
Knowlton, Janitor Spencer and others
had reached the office door a man and a
woman struggling violently for the pos
session of a pistol came into the passage
and continued their scuffling. The ap
pearance of several men caused the man
to throw down his pistol and make his
escape as - ion as possible. The lady re
tired into the attorney's office.
The woman, who declares she came
near being murdered, is Miss Eliza
Schomlu-rg, a servant in some family at
Frultvale. Her assailant was Frank
Mott, a gripman on the San Pablo avenue
road. The details of the incident, as told
by Attorney Aldrich, make it very plain
that jc-alousy is at the bottom of the at
tempted tragedy.
Miss Schomberg' met Mott several years:
ago and the two have been very Intimate
friends. The lady loaned Mott $20, and
recently he asked for an additional loan
of $200.
Miss Schomberg being acquainted with
Attorney Aldrich called on him this noon
to discuss the advisability of loaning her
lover any more money. The attorney was
out, and while the woman was waiting
Frank Mott entered the office. He at once
demanded of her that she should con
tinue to be as good a friend to him as j
during the last ten years. 'From his lan
guage he seemed to Infer that taking
legal advice as to the loan was equivalent
to casting him aside. Miss Schomberg
says that he produced a pistol and told
her that if she were thinking of leaving <
him he would kill her and then kill him
self on the spot. She rushed at him, at
tempting to grab the pistol, and being a
powerful woman she was able to prevent !
him using it until she had pushed him I
into the hall, when Others came to her aid. !
The struggle was very tierce, as was
shown by the fact that the pistol was
"broken" during the contest and some of i
the cartridges fell on the Boor, where they
were found by Attorney Aidrich. In his ;
flight Mott picked up his gun and took it
with him. Reminiscences of the struggle ,
were seen all over the office of the attor
ney, and some of the bystanders took
away fragments of the lady's broken
hair comb as souvenirs of the occasion.
Frank Mott was arrested to-night and
brought to the City Prison, charged with
carrying a concealed weapon, His bail
was fixed at $100, which be was unable to I
furnish. Mis version of the affair at At
torney Aldrich'a office this afternoon va
ries materially from the story told by
Miss Schomberg.
"Tills woman has been pestering the j
life out of me to marry her. " said Mott,
"and because I cannot see my way clear
to accommodate her she is trying to make
trouble for me. We have been very Inti
mate friends, but, you see, I've got a
wife and two children back in Illinois, so
I couldn't very well marry Miss Schom
berg. My wife and I separated several
years asm because we' could not get along
well together.
"Miss Schomberg has been trying to in
duce me to secure a divorce, and this aft
ernoon she went to Attorney Aldrich's
office to ascertain whether I had com- '
menced proceedings. In doing this she
interfered with my affairs and I got sick ;
of it, and so I followed her up and made
a bluff to scare her off. I had my revol
ver, unloaded, in my pocket. I did not
draw it. and made no attempt to injure
her, but just as soon as she saw me she ;
save a scream and ran out of the office.
1 have never asked her for a cent, but she
has frequently demanded money of me.
If she intends to make further trouble for
me in this way I guess I can fire back
and make trouble for her as well."

THE REAPER CLAIMS A
PIONEER ARCHITECT
WILLIAM PATTON DIES AT HIS
HOME IN ALAMEDA.
ALAMEDA, May 23.â William Patton,
one of the pioneer architects of the Pa
cific Coast, who designed a portion of
San Francisco's new City Hall, died this
morning at his home. 2253 Central avenue,
Alameda. He had been ill for a number
of months, suffering from cancer of the
throat, and his death had been expected
daily. Deceased was a native of Sunder
land, England, aged 7S years. When a boy
he entered an architect's office and served
his apprenticeship. When news of the
gold discoveries in California reached the
old country he wa.s among the first to
start for the new El Dorado. He came
around the Horn in the same vessel with
Thomas Blythe, and Die two were fast
friends up to the time of the latter's
death.
After passing several years in the mines
Mr. Patton returned to San Francisco
and opened an architect's office on Wash
ington street, where he followed his pro
â¢ n with great success and amassed
a fortune, part of which, however, was
lost in late years by unprofitable specu
lation. Mr. Patton married in San Fran
cis* <>. About twenty years ago he built
his home at 2853 Central avenue and took
up his residence in Alameda.
Many of 'San Francisco's early land
marks were designed by Mr. Patton.
Some of them have since been turn down
while others are still standing monu
ments to the architect's skill. Among
the San Francisco buildings designed by
him are the Sutter-street Synagogue the
Safe Deposit building, Donahue block.
Mission and Fremont streets; the old
Starr King church, since replaced by the
new structure; thp old chronicle^buliding,
Hush and Kearny streets, and me Alca
zar building. He was supervising archi
tect of the new City Hall, and designer
of the northwest and northeast wings, the
City Prison and the Hall of Records.
Many of the tlnr-st buildings in Alameda
were also designed by him.
Mrs. Patton died. about fourteen yearn
ago. Two sons, William and Clarence
Patton, survive the father. Deceased was
a member of the Society of California
Pioneers. The funeral services will be
held under the auspices of the society.

STRUCK BY A LOCAL.
His Pegleg Caught and Aged Edward
Walker Fell in Front of the
Locomotive.
OAKLAND, May Edward Walker,
aged 79 years and an inmate of the San
Francisco Almshouse, was struck by the
new broadgauge local at the narrow
gauge mole this afternoon and may die
from his injuries.
Walker's left leg consists of a stump
fixed in a peg strapped to his thigh. In
some manner this peg became fastened
between the boards near the track and
Walker fell in front of the locomotive.
At the Receiving Hospital he was treat
ed by Dr. Stratton for a fractured left
thigh and arm and numerous serious
bruises about the head. Though he seemed
to be conscious all the while his chances
for recovery are considered meager owing
to his advanced age. Walker stated In
whispers that he had intended walking to
Santa Clara.

FAIR CASE DELAYED.
Supreme Court Ordered thÂ© Appeals
Resubmitted.
After all the litigation as to the two
â wills of James Q. Fair, there Is yet an
other delay. Yesterday the Supreme
Court made the following order:
Charles L. Fair vs. James S. Ag^ius
et al.
In the ahove entitled appeals, one
of the Justices of this court not hav
ing hoard the arguments and there
fore being disqualified to participate in
the decisions thereof, and it being
deemed important that all the Jus
tices should participate. It is order, d
that the submission of said appeals be
set aside and that they be resubmitted
to the full court. As the briefs on
these appeals are very elaborate and
as several of the oral arguments made
at the hearing have been printed and
are on file, counsel for the several
parties may stipulate if they so desire
to immediately resubmit the cases on
the briefs and printed arguments nov;
on file; otherwise they will be placed
for argument on the next July calen
dar.
The order is lucid on Its face and re
fers to the fact that Justice Van Dyke,
who succeeded Justice Van Fleet, did not
officially hear the argument in the ap
peals. Hence their resubmlssion. There
is little doubt in the minds of attorneys
but that counsel will agree to stipulate
for the resubmlssion on the briefs on
file.
READY TO PARADE.
Men of the Naval Militia Will Come
to the City on Decoration Day.
The Santa Cruz- division of the Naval
Militia will take part in the parade on
Decoration day. There will be at least
fifty men who will come up on a special
train and with the two divisions from this
city and the men from the engineer divi
sion they will help to make the biggest
turnout the Naval Militia has so far put
upon the street. There will be a battery
of artillery from the divisions from this
cityâ four guns and over a hundred men
and officersâ and several sections of in
fantry.
It was as artillery the Naval Militia first
offer. itself to the Government at the
outbreak of the war, and the battery
which will turn out on Decoration day
will be the formation that was offered at
that time. Each of the four guns will
be manned by twenty-four men, three
petty officers and a commissioned officer,
and altogether they will make a formid
able formation. The guns are the latest
Hotchkiss one-pounders and good at a
range of 2500 yards. â¢ â¢
The detachment will be under the com
| mand of Captain L. 11. Turner and Lieu
tenant Commander T. A. Nerney.

Union Men Strike.
Organized labor is having trouble with
the firm of Campbell <fc Pettls, contractors
for the brick work on the new training
school being erected on Goat Island.
Walking Delegate Saundera ascertained
that the eight-hotir law was being vio
lated and caused warrants to be Issued
for th- arrests of the offenders. The la
bor unions reported the violation to La
bor Commissioner Fitzgerald, who went
to the Island and found that skilled labor
had been dispensed with and non-union
labor employed Instead. On the Btrength
of the troubles all of the union men at
work on the job went out on a strike yes
terday.
Slashed With a Razor.
Lizzie Smith, a colored woman living at
303 i'ost street, was taken to the Receiv
ing Hospital yesterday morning to have
a long deep wound in each arm stitched
and dressed. She and two colored men
had been drinking all night in a house on
luish and Dupont streets, when a row
started and one of the men drew a razor
and attempted to cut Lizzie's thoat. She
threw up her arms to protect her throat.
When asked for the name of the razor
wielder she said that she would not tell
and would die game. She was sent home.
Slapped Madarne's Face.
Mme. Ayeesha Bentenye of Little
Egypt's troupe, performing at a theater
on Market street, swore to a complaint
In Judge Grahani - .s court yesterday for
the arrest of Ed Homan, proprietor of
the theater, on the charge of battery.
Madame asserts that Sunday she and lit
tle Egypt had a dispute and Homan came
upon the scene. He accused the madame
â  ! being jealous of Little Egypt and
slapped her face real hard. Homan was
arrested and released on cash ball.
Printer Dyer Still Missing.
The fate of C. E. Dyer, the missing
printer supposed to have been drowned,
is still involved in obscurity. Coroner
Hill received a letter yesterday from a
relative of Dyer at Angels ("amp. Cala
veras County, stating that the knife and
spoon on the person of the unknown
drowned man found in the bay last week
were not those belonging to Dyer. In the
meantime Dyer remains missing.

NOTICE Is hereby Riven by order of the Board
of Directors of the OCEANIC STEAMSHIP
COMPANY that a meeting of the stockholders
of said company has been called by said
Board, to be held on FRIDAY, the second day
of June. A. D. 1599, at 11 o'clock in the tore-
noon of said day, at the principal place of
business of paid Company, at the building
where the said Board of Directors usually
meets, namely, at the office of Bald Company,
number 327 Market st., In the City and County
of San Francisco, State of California; that
the object of said meeting Is to consider and
act upon the proposition that said Company
create a bonded indebtedness of two million
five hundred thousand dollars ($2,500,000), in
United states gold coin, for the purpose of
raising money to complete the construction
of its steamships and their equipment, for
use In the business of this corporation, and
'to purchase and pay for any other property
within the purposes of this Company: and to
secure the bonded Indebtedness so proposed
to be created by a mortgage upon Its steam
and calling: ships and all other property of
said company now owned or hereafter to be
acquired by said Company.
By order of the Board of Directors of the
Oceanic .Steamship Company.
[Corporate Seal.] B. H. SHELDON,
Secretary of the Oceanic Steamship Com-
pany.

PTATE PRISON SUPPLIES.
SEALED PROPOSALS will be received at
the office of the Clerk at State Prison. SAN
QUENTIN, until 1<1;XO a m. SATURDAY,
10TH DAY OF JUNE, 1899.
And at the office of the Clerk at State Prison,
FOLSOM. until 9:30 a. m. SATURDAY,
17TH DAY OF JUNE, IÂ»'9.
At which times and places they will be
opened In presence of bidders, for furnishing
and delivering supplies for said prisons, con-
sisting of subsistence stores, forage, fuel,
clothing, medicines, lime, cement and other
staple supplies, from July 1. 1899, to June 30,
1900, inclusive, on conditions as per printed
schedule, which will be furnished upon ap-
plication at either of the above offices.
No bid will be considered unless made upon
said schedules, nor unless accompanied by
the certified check therein required, nor from
any other parties than bona fide dealers in
the classes of articles to be furnished. Each
bidder must accompany his bid with a certi-
fied check upon some well-known and re-
sponsible banking house for at least 10 per
cent of the amount, payable to R. M. Fitz-
gerald, President of the Board, of his bid,
conditioned that the bidder will enter into a
contract upon notice of acceptance as re-
quired by law.
Bids for either prison must be marked sepa-
rately. All payments made on contracts will
be paid In cash or Controller's warrants.
Bidders will carefully follow the conditions
of the schedules.
The Board reserves the right to reject any
or all bids as the public interest may re-
quire, and to order any quantity over or under
amount specified.
EL M. FITZGERALD, President.
J. V. ELLIS, Clerk.
PROPOSALS for Supplies of Hardware, Drugs,
Lumber, etc.. for Fire Department.
Office of the Clerk of the Board of Super-
visors of the 'City and County of San Fran-
cisco, May 23, lbSSt.
In accordance with Resolution No. 2633
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Supervisors,
SEALED PROPOSALS will be received in
open session of the Board of Supervisors on
MONDAY AFTERNOON. June 5. ISW. from !
2:30 to 3 o'clock, for furnishing supplies for
usa of the Fire Department, from July 1.
1899. to June 30, 1901. In accordance with
schedule, embracing drugs, ironwork, hard-
ware, general supplies, paints, paint brushes,
oils and lumber, prepared by the Committee
on Fire Department of the Board of Supervis-
ors, on file In this office, copies of which may
be obtained at the office of the Clerk of the
Board of Supervisors, on second floor, City
Hall.
NOTlCEâ Bidders will estimate separately
for each article enumerated, as per denomi-
nation, with the distinct understanding that
the lowest and best bid for each article re-
quired will be accepted, and that in every in-
stance no bid except from persons or firms
who are regular recognized dealers In the
articles enumerated and bid upon will be re-
ceived. Also, all articles and supplies shall
lie of the same kind, quality and texture a.-
per samples kept and stored In the Corpora-
tion Yard of the Fire Department, at No. 50
Sacramento street. AI6O the right is reserved
that the Chief Engineer of the Fire Depart-
ment, by and with the consent of the Com-
mittee on Fire Department of the Board of
Supervisors, shall have power to purchase
any or all of the supplies required, at the
market rates, at the expense of the party
to whom any contract may be awarded to
furnlFh the same. If, upon the requisition of
the officers named, the party falls or omits
to furnish the quantity or quality of the sup-
plies designated, which, under contract, he
may be required to furnish. Also, all articles
and supplies furnished to be subject to the
approval of the Chief Engineer of the Fire
Department, the Board of Fire Commission-
ers and the Committee on Fire Department of
the Board of Supervisors, whose decision shall
be final and conclusive as to the reception
or rejection of any or all of the articles or
supplies offered.
The party or parties to whom each one of
the contracts may be awarded will be re-
quired, prior to or at the time of the execu-
tion of said contracts, to pay the cost of ad-
vertising this notice In three dally news-
papers, and each successful bidder to also
pay the cost of the publication in the offi-
cial newspaper of the Resolution awarding
him his contract. Each article awarded to
be charred with its pro rata of the cost of
advertising this notice.
In order to preserve uniformity and to fa-
cilitate the award the Board hae resolved to
receive no bids unless made upon blank forms
prepared by the Committee, and a certified
check on some bank In the City and County
of San Francisco for the sum of five hundred
($500) dollars, In favor of and made payable
to tne Clerk of the Board of Supervisors,
must be deposited by the bidder, with his
bid conditioned if the proposal Is accepted
and the contract awarded; and if the bidder
shall fall or neglect to pay the printing
charges, execute the contract and give the
bond required within six days after the
award is made, then and in that case the
said sum shall be paid Into the City and
County Treasury by said Clerk as liquidated
damages for such failure and neglect.
Blanks furnished by the Clerk.
The Board reserves the right to reject all
bldÃ If the public good so require.
JNO. A. RUSSELL. Clerk.

GONE, AND HIS BOOKS
MAY BE $10,000 SHORT
E. F. Spaulding, Cashier of the Fireman's
Fund Insurance Company, Unac
countably Prolongs His Vaca
tion Without Leave.
THE accounts of. Edward F.
SpauJding, assistant cashier of
the Fireman's Fund Insurance
Company of California, are nol
straight. Yesterday experts
found discrepancies in his books and
It is surmised that a shortage approxi
mating. $10,000 may be traced.
The assistant cashier should have
been at his desk in the company's of
fice at the corner of California and
Sansome streets last Monday morning.
He was due then to return to duty
Bfter his annual vacation covering a.
period of ten days. His Failure to ap
pear or report a reason for his ab
sence caused D. J. Staples, president
of the company, to direct an expert ex
amination of Mr. Bpaulding'a books.
The president was seen at his resi
dence, 711 Taylor street, last evening
and responding to Inquiries on behalf
of The Call said:
"In this affair the company has noth
ing to conceal from the public. 1 will
ray with perfect frankness that we
have found some discrepancies in the
books of Mr. Spaulding, our assistant
cashier. The report that 120,000 is miss-
Ing is all nonsense. We do not know
yet "what the amount involved is. but
do know that it cannot exceed $10,000.
It Is likely to be less than half that
amount. The fact that Mr. Spaulding
did not come to the office yesterday
caused us to look over his accounts and
some discrepancies were found.
"He went away on his annual vaca
tion recently. There was nothing ir
regular in his going away. The vaca
tion was due him according to the cus
tom of the office, which gives to the
fifty or sixty young men employed
there an annual outing of ten days. He
should, however, have reported at his
desk yesterday (Monday) morning, but
he did not appear or send word when
he would come. It was then suggested
that an examination should be made of
his books, and I say frankly that some
discrepancies were discovered. Come
to the office of the company to-morrow
and I may be able to state definitely
the extent of the discrepancies. No,
there has been no thought of placing

Mr. Spaulding."
When asked concerning the place
where Mr. Spaulding went for his va
cation Mr. Staples replied: "I do not ',
know where ho went. We scarcely in
qUire where an employe enjoys his va- ,
cation, ['understand that some of them
do not go out of the city, but stay about
home and do ;i little gardening. If a
man does not return when his vacation
expires we make inquiry concerning: his
absence. The system of allowing a va
cation Is so conducted that each man's
: for going and returning is regis- j
tered."
President Staples was asked concern
ing surety bonds to protect the com
pany from loss. Replying to this line
of inquiry, he remarked:
"We have never required bonds of |
our employes. Our business, fire and !
marine, amounts to a million dollars a !
year. For thirty-three years 1 have
l>een at the head of the company, and j
during that entire period only two de- j
faults have occurred. Our system is |
such that an extensive loss in this re- j
spect is impossible. Our cashier, Mr. i
Wood, is away at present, and Mr. j
Gardner is acting as cashier. Mr.
Spaulding is onh- the assistant.
"Yes, he is the son of that grand old
man, K. W. Spaulding. who was United
States Assistant Treasurer, Mayor of
< >akland and a prominent member of
the Republican State Central Commit
tee. If the son has gone wrong, the !
blow will be a sad one to the father,
who is an honored citizen and a most
excellent man. The assistant cashier,
Spaulding, has been in our employ
about seven years."

CAPTAIN DAN
HASKELL DEAD
A WELL KNOWN PILOT AND
TOWBOAT MAN.
1 Was for Years Master of the Tug's
Alert, Belief and Fearless and
Later Became a Pilot.
Captain Daniel H. Haskell, the well
known master mariner and pilot, died at
Ills home. 1694 Page street, yesterday af
ter a lingering illness. He began to fall
nearly two years aeo, but it waa only
within the last monih or six weeks that
he to his bed. Then dropsy set In
and gradually worked Its way up to liis
heart.
Captain Haskell was one of the best
known towboat men on the Pacifiv <'<..tst.
Years ago he began his career as deck
hand on the tug Neptune. Then hÂ« was
captain of the Pa.-inV Mall tug Milieu
Crirtitli. When John D. Spreckels went

Haskell was sent to Tramps' to briiiK her

until the big tug Fearless was built.
When Captain Haskell had been In com-
I mand of the Fearless for a number of
i years there became a vacancy among the
pilots. Captain Trask retired and Captain
Haskell was unanimously elected to suc
c-eed him. During his career Captain
Haskell never met with an accident and
was one of the most successful men In
tbe business at picking up derelicts and
i bringing In salvage .-hips.
The Fearless, with which Captain Has
; kill was so long Identified, was sold to the

the dispatch-boat Iroquoiw. The- Relief

also The Millen (4rifflth. while the old
Neptane went to the "boneyard" long
ago.
Captain Haskell leaves a host of friends

much missed in shipping circles.

GIRLS' HIGH SCHOOL.. May 20 1899.
Editor Callâ Dear Sir: An article appeared

Girls' High Is not Inferior in any respect to the
other.

that the Girls' High School has been regularly
accredited since. 1890, and the last reports gives
the following subjects in which it was fully

"Mr. Spaulding was the assistant

Inquiry was made in Oakland last

that the assistant cashier had fled. Ac

er's wife, E. F. Spaulding and his fam
ily moved to Oakland a short time ago
and took up their residence at 1664
Webster street. Mrs. Spaulding. the
sister-in-law mentioned, declared that
she does not know where her husband's
brother has gone, more than that he
left home a week or ten days ago to
take his usual vacation. She stated
that he was accompanied by his wife.
Inquiry as to the direction taken by
the couple was of no avail, the lady
claiming that she knew nothing of
Spaulding's business or where he and
his wife had gone for their vacation.
An inspection of the house last occu
pied by the Spauldings at 1664 Webster
street indicated that it had been closed
for sevetal days, and the neighbors
seemed to be In ignorance of the
whereabouts or movements of the last
occupants. The family had gone was
all they seemed to know about -the
matter, and the time of the departure
was variously estimated at from one
to two weeks.
Thomas M. Gardiner, cashier of the
Fireman's Fund Insurance Company,
t^p whose department Mr. Spaulding
was attached, resides on the heights of
East Oakland, overlooking the estuary.

ECLECTIC MEDICOS
IN ANNUAL SESSION
The twenty-sixth annual meeting of the
Eclectic Medical Society opened its ses
sion in College Hall at 1422 Folsom street
terday afternoon. The medical fra
ternity of the interior was well represent
ed and the papers read were notable for
new thoughts and sound reasoning: After
President Charles Mealand, M. D., of Sac
ramento delivered his annual address
Mayor Plielan welcomed the visiting med
icos and in a few words paid his respects
to the gentlemen and the noble profession
which they represent. At the conclusion
of the Mayor's little speech the members
of the association paid him a standing
vote of thanks.

A NEGRO COOK
RUNS AMUCK ON
THE AUSTRALIA
Shot a Waiter and
Was Shot Himself.
WAS DRIVEN CRAZY bY DRINK
WOUNDED AND DYING TAKEN
BACK TO HONOLULU.
The Steamer Was Under Way for San
Francisco When the Lunatic
Began Using His Pistol
With Deadly Effect.
George Wade, chief cook of the steamer
Australia, ran amuck shortly after the
vessel left Hawaii on May 16. He armed
himself with a bulldog revolver and fatal-
The Australia's Murderous Cook and His Victim.
ly wounded G. W. Gelesple, one of the
waiters, drove a number of the crew Into
i hiding, and the only thing that stopped
i his wild career was a bullet from a Wln
j Chester rirle in the hands of Harry Evans,
Chief of Police of Honolulu.
A man named Sherman victimized near
ly all the business men in Honolulu and
finally succeeded In yetting $150 out of
Chief Justice Judd. The latter was on
the bench, when he heard that Sherman
was trying to get away on the Australia,
so he at once issued a warrant for his
arrest and adjourned court to see that
the document was served. That was how
it happened that Chief of Police Evans
was aboard the steamer. He went out
with the vessel, intending to return with
the pilot, and was searching for his man
in company with Purser McCombe and
<'hief Steward Seeley when the shooting
I began.
The Australia sailed from Honolulu at
i 4 p. m. About half an hour before that
! time Chief Cook Wade had gone aboard
i drunk and quarrelsome. He was sent to
his room and told to take a good sleep.
He was not seen again until 1:35 p. m.,
when the Australia was outside the reef
and the pilot was preparing to leave the
ship. At tha.t time Wade accosted Harry
! Heed in the port alleyway and said he was
i going to "do up" somebody. At that
moment Chief Steward Seeley passed, and
Wade glared after him and said to Reed,
"I'll kill that fellow before the day is
out." Keed tried to persuade the drunken
cook to go back to his room, but Wade
drove him off with the pistol.
John I. Taylor, the baker, was the next
to run across the crazy man. Wade near
ly scared him to death by firing off the
pistol and asking him: "Are you with
me? I'm going to kill half this ship's
; crew." "I'm with you! I'm with you."
said Taylor, and then ran for his life.
I Sydney C. Boyd. the butcher, came along
â  and he did not bandy any words but
struck Wade a blow that dazed htm for a
moment. Wade made a rush at Boyd
and the latter grasped his arm to pre
; vent him from shooting, and in the scuffle
the pistol went off. Gelespie and Thomas
i Breslin went to Boyd's assistance, but
1 the crazy n'-gro was more than a match
j for them. He got his iirm free and shot
i Gelespie in the left thigh, shattering the
I bone. The wounded man fell to the floor
: and Boyd and Breslin ran into ihe engi
neer's room.
When the two men ran Wade gathered
himself together and, looking down at
Gelespie as lie lay on the deck, said:
"Sydney, you struck me, and I'm going:
to kill you."
"I'm not Sydney." groaned Gelespie. '
"I don't care; you're one of the gang,
and I'm goint; to kill you."- yelled Wade.
With that he fired, the bullet striking
' Gelespie in the left groin and passed
through his hip. Wade then retired to
j his room and reloaded his revolver.
When the cook came out again he pro
j ceeded along the alley in search of Chief
: Steward Seeley. When he reached the
galley he saw Chief of Police Evans on
i the starboard side of the ship and took a
I shot at him. In the meantime, Chief
' Officer Lawless, who was forward, had
â  been Informed of the shooting, and he at
j once reported to Captain Houdlette. The
: latter counseled caution, but told the chief
i officer to secure the maniac at all haz
ards. Lawless went to his room, loaded
his Winchester and thus armed started
I out to look for Wade. On the way he met
Chief of Police Evans, and the latter took
the gun out of Lawless' hands. When
BUTCHERS WILL
PICNIC TO - DAY
AT SHELL MOUND
An Enjoyable Outing
Is Anticipated.
THOUSANDS WILL ATTEND
SPEEDY HORSES WILL COKTfETE
FOR BIG PURSES.
Well-Known Meat-Dealers to Handle
the Ribbons, With Heavy Bet
ting on the Various
Events.
Woe will betide the houperrife who did
not lay In her supply of meat yesterday.
This Is hutrhfrs' day, and all the
knights of the cleaver and their employes
and families are to celebrate. Every
butcher-shop in this city and also In Oak
land, Berkeley and Alameda is closed, and
the entire meat trade, from the cattle
king to the boys hauling hides In the
smallest tanyard, are prepared to join In
the reunion that yearly brings the mem
bers of the Butchers' Board of Trade and
their friends together.
This Is the sixth celebration that the
butchers have indulged in. It marks the
sixth year of their declaration of inde
pendence of the meat barons, and to them
tells of a long and hard fight against the
millions of a trust that has gripped the
butcher-shops of every large city in the
United States except San Francisco.
This year's celebration is to be even
more elaborate than usual. There are to
be horse races at the Oakland track and
dancing, foot races and games at Shell
Mound. One ticket will admit the bearer
to both pleasure parks and also give the
picnicker a chance at a large, miscellan
eous collection of gate prizes, chief of
which is a purse of $100 in gold. The sec
ond prize, which Is to be of five $10 gold
pieces, will also be worth carving horn*.
An unusual feature of the faces will be
two dashes of professional runners, for
which the Butchers' Board of Trade Is to
hang up interesting purses. The entries
for these races are as follows:
One mile dashâFaversham 96, Alvin E
113, Dare II 116, Maginis 109, The Plunger
111. Alvero 113.
Three-quarter mile dashâPueniah 97
Homestake 109, Florence Fink 97, Somis 97
Crawford 111. Tom Smith 111, Silver State
109, Loch Ness 114, Master Lee 97, Road
warmer 111, Durward 111. Spry Lark 10!)
Uncle True 109, Inverness II 114.
Of course, there are to be races for the
butchers, races for the vaqueros and
races for the butcher boys. As there Is
much pride felt in their horses and their
skill as ' drivers by the different retail
merchants, the races In which they par
ticipate will have a double interest.
Last year's crowd exceeded 24,000. Even
more people are expected this year, since
more preparations have been made to en
tertain them and more opportunities are
offered for them spending every hour of
the day In enjoyment.

Wade saw them coming he fired his pistol
and the bullet whistled between the two
men, making both of them duck. Evans
did not give him time to lire again, but,
raising the Winchester, sent a bullet
crashing into Wade's head. It struck the
frontal bone and was turned aside. plow
ing its way through the bone and flesh,
making a deep furrow. ,;:. -
The insane cook was only partly
stunned, and In spite of his.wound he had
to be handcuffed and manacled before he
would keep quiet. In the meantime Dr.
H. J. McNulty. the ship's surgeon, had
been in attendance upon Gelespie and had
dressed his wounds. He then went to the
cook, but could do nothing for him. Both
the wounded men were taken on deck
lowered Into the pilot boat and taken
ashore. '

BAPTISTS HERE
TO ATTEND THE
BIG CONVENTION
Hundred and Fifty in
the Party.
The two special trains from Boston and
Chicago, scheduled to arrive in this city
last evening at 6:30 o'clock with dele
gates to the convention of National Bap
tist Societies to be held in this city for
the next eight days, did not arrive at the
Mole till midnight. Nearly all the dele
gates remained in the cars over night,
and will be brought to this side early this
morning.
There are 150 people in the party, and
half of them will go to the Occidental,
while the other half will take quarters
at the Pleasanton. The rest of the dele
gates from the southern part of the State,
Oregon and Washington will arrive to
day.
This is the first time the Baptist Soci
eties have celebrated their anniversaries
west of the Rockies, ami the sixty-sev
enth celebration promises to be a notable
one in the history of the church. The
First Church of this city has been select
ed as the place of meeting. The seating
capacity of the church is eight hundred.
The edifice has been decorated In ever
greens, flowers and flags by W. B.
Thompson, and presents a pleasing ap
pearance. The Sunday school rooms have
been fitted up for exhibits from the vari
ous churches. Arrangements have been
made at the different hotels and boarding
houses for three thousand people who will
bo here during the convention.
The meetings will commence at 10 a. m.
to-day, when the Woman's Baptist
Home Mission Society will celebrate Its
twenty-second anniversary. There will be
another session of the same society at
2pm Both will be held in the large au
dience room of the church, on the north
side of Eddy street, west of Jones. \Yntle
the ladies are celebrating their anniver
sary at the church there will be a Pacific
Coast conference In the Interest of higher
education in the auditorium of the Young
Men* Christian Association, corner of
Mason and Kills streets, at 10 a. m. and
1-30 p m. These meetings and those to be
held in the church are open to both sexes
and are free to all. This evening
at the church the American Baptist Edu
cational Society will celebrate its eleventh
anniversary with an address by Dr. Gam
brell of Texas, a report by Dr. Morehouse
of Xew York, and an address by Dr.
Brownson of California College.
